Vol. 26 NOVEMBER-DECEMBEB. 1948 No. 6 
Enrollment Reac 
New Record; 

With enrollment reaching a new high of 5,090 students, the 1948-49 schoolycar offi­cially got under way on Sept. 16 following 
^ three days of registration. 
The current enrollment exceeds by 323 the former top figure of 4,767, set a year ago. But a midyear graduation class of more than 400 will materially reduce the student population at the start of the second semes­ter in February. (Because the midyear grad­uates are so numerous. Commencement ceremonies for them will be conducted on Saturday and Sunday, Jan. 29 and 30.) 
Each of the 48 states, the District of Co­lumbia, Puerto Rico and six foreign coun­tries arc represented in the class of approxi­mately 1,279 freshmen who began the year Significant of the rapidly changing times is the fact that only slightly more than 10 per cent of the new class are veterans of World War II. (The whole student body is one-half veteran.) 
Again this year, registration was carried on in the wide spaces of the Drill Hall on the campus, and with wonderfully simpli­
% ficd and coordinated mechanics of operation. Gone almost completely are one-after-anoth­cr long lines of enrollment typical of earlier years. Instead, some students were able this year to complete their entire registration procedure in five minutes; many others took only a little longer. 
Credit for the new system goes largely this year to Rev. Howard Kenna, C.S.C., direc­tor of studies, and to Rev. James E. Norton, C.S.C., his assistant. Rev. John J. Lane, C.S.C., now dean of Kings College, Wilkes-Barrc, Pa., assistant director of studies at Notre Dame for several years, worked with Father Kenna a year ago in installing the new registration procedure. 
Identification pictures of all new students were, again this year, taken in the Drill Hall on registration days. University regula­tions require that all students carry "I.D. cards" for identification purposes and to provide admittance to athletic contests. 
^ ^ With members of the Student Council, 

hes 5,090 for 1,279 Freshmen 
giving magnificent aid, the new students were this year introduced to Notre Dame more quickly, warmly and completely than ever before. During registration, informa­tion desks were set up at strategic spots on the campus and in the freshman residence halls, and tours of the campus were con­ducted at appointed hours for freshmen and their families. This latter undertaking prov­ed to be singularly successful. It was carried out with the generous aid of many members of the faculty and the administration staff. 
The huge new class became acquainted with Washington Hall on Monday evening, Sept. 13, when they were officially greeted by Rev. John J. Cavanaugh, C.S.C., presi­dent, and by Dean Lawrence H. Baldinger of the College of Science. The traditional Knute Rocknc film followed. 
On Tuesday, most of the freshmen began a series of aptitude and placement tests given under the direction of Edward R. Quinn, '28, head of the University's testing division. Started last year, this testing program for freshmen was greatly extended this year. Two programs of orientation talks followed for freshmen on Tuesday evening. And on Wednesday evening, preceding the start of classes Thursday morning, the new men got together for a cokes-and-doughnuts mixer in the Gym. Entertainment, cheers, talks by members of the Monogram Club and gath­erings by states added up to a highly re­garded session. 
All the new Notre Dame men, including the sizable group who had to stay in the Oliver Hotel for a while—until room condi­tions became stabilized on the campus—^were ready to take over. 
Educators from many parts of the United States and from Canada, South America and Europe were among 47 new teachers

What he described as "the deliberate ex-\ Volume 26, No. 6, Nov.-Dec, 1948 
ploitation of the public school system at the expense of the private, religious school" was 

ffH. deplored on Sept. 19 by Father John Cava­naugh, president, in an address at the sol­emn opening of the schoolyear at Notre Dame. 
This, he charged, is the "natural corollary of the campaign to exclude God and reli­gion from American life." 
"Skillfully the impression is given," he said, "that the public school system was established by the founding fathers in 1776, with the Declaration of Independence; that all great Americans worth mentioning have been products of the public school system; that the philosophy of Americanism in its pristine vitality and beauty is preserved and communicated chiefly in the public schools; that in the public schools young men and women are taught to look with singular re­spect to the American flag, to sing the patri­otic songs, and to risk their lives in time of national danger with special abandon; that, in other words, the public school system is the exclusive and necessary melting pot of the races and colors and creeds that make 
W up American life." 
Father Cavanaugh emphasized that he was not attacking the public school system itself, but only "those who would pervert their nature and use support of the public schools as an argument against the support of private schools." 
"All of this propaganda might be toler­able," he declared, "if it were not intended to discredit private, religious education; to make its patriotism suspect and open to question the value of the contribution that private, religious education makes to the so­ciety of which it is a part." 
Pointing out that for the first seventy years of the American Republic there were no public schools in this country. Father Cavanaugh stated that Washington, Jeffer­son, Madison, the Adamses, Franklin, Car­roll and other great American patriots of that period were graduates of private, reli­gious schools. The first public schools were not established in the United States until the 1840's, he added. 
"Who communicated the philosophy of Americanism in the interval, if not the pri­vate, religious schools?" he asked. 
"Nevertheless, our system of public in­struction is pointed out as the continuing channel of the American government, al­though how this thesis can be maintained is beyond me. The Declaration of Indepen­dence states categorically that the existence of God, the Creator, is a necessary presup­position of our form of government. Every state constitution and several decisions of the Supreme Court emphasize the religious nature of the philosophy of American gov­ernment. I have no doubt that many teach­ers in our public school system, even after the Supreme Court decision in the McCol­lum case, will still point out the religious nature of American government, but they will be breaking the law if they persist in such teaching." 
Father Cavanaugh said he did not ques­tion the patriotism of public school students and graduates, but he wanted to emphasize the "love of country is certainly no less a shining virtue among the students of our private, religious schools." 
"Nor, do I like to see the public school system held up as the only melting pot in ."Vraerica. If the record means anything, the work of merging the different races and creeds goes on so effectively in the private schools, that in war and peace the patriotism of private school students and graduates is second to none. The last war and the wars before proved conclusively that private school products melted easily into the Ma­rines, and the Army and Navy; the records of peace show the products of private schools being absorbed as easily into the professions and business and civil life as the products of the public schools. 
"It seems quite clear, then, that although there may be justification for supporting the public school system by the taxes of this country, that justifiaction is not in the claim that the public schools arc the sole melting pot, the exclusive cradle of American patri­otism and the purveyor of the American philosophy of government. 
"Many modem voices would have us think that a family has a duty to send its children to a public school, and that only by a kind of privilege or dispensation may it commit them to a private, religious school. I should like it known that an integral part of the American freedom is the freedom of the family to select the school to which its chil­dren are to be sent. The state can justly make demands only when the family fails to provide its children the kind of education which will make good American citizens. 
".. . I always smile at the reluctance of some in this country to give free text books or rides in school busses to Catholic boys and girls. Justice demands that Catho­lics be freed from double taxation, or that they receive some consideration for the funds they pour in as taxes to support public edu­cation. Against this contention it is some­times argued that there is no practical way of excluding from taxation the Catholics and Lutherans and Episcopalians who oper­ate their own school systems. Well, a practi­cal way is found through taxation of gasa­line to make those who use our highways pay for their construction and maintenance. I think a way can be found if this problem is honestly understood :and considered in a spirit of fairness. 
"If the good of the country demands the exclusive development of the public school system with funds provided by taxing all the citizens, if public education must be non­religious, as the Supreme Court requires, then the further secularization of America must follow as night follows day. Those who believe that American life must never be completely secularized, that we need to work back in the other direction to the religious faith of our fathers, to the union of every­day affairs with morality and religion must elect to provide private, religious schools." 
The testing program for ireshmen was much enlarged this year. Freshmen get manr a test bat also (at nmcer) cokes and doughnata.

UNIVERSITY 
By EDWABD A. nSCHEH, '37 
TOP LABOR LEADERS William Green, president of the A. F. of L. and Philip Mur­ray, president of the CIO, accepted appoint­ments as members of the Ad\nsor>- Council of the College of Commerce. The announce­ment was made when the Council met here Sept. 25, 26. Their acceptances bring the membership of the council to 31. The group was organized early in the year to establish closer ties between the College of Commerce and business, industr>-, and labor. 
NEW HEAD of the Department of Re­ligion is the Rev. Theodore M. Hesburgh, 
C.S.C. He succeeds the Rev. Roland G. Simonitsch. C.S.C., who has been trans­ferred to Holy Cross College, Washing­ton. D. C. In addition to teaching at Holy Cross, Father Simonitsch will study at Catholic University of America. 
AN EIGHT-POINT PROGRAM to help achieve industrial peace betiveen labor and management was proposed by Prof. John 
H. Shcehan, head of the Department of Economics, at a meeting of the Notre Dame Club of the Moha%vk Valley, Utica, N. Y. 
The proposals were: 1) \ formally-organized, sincerely operated labor relations division for each large business. 2) Better-trained union personnel for company rela­tions work. 3) Elimination by both manage­ment and labor of "yes" men to improve "critical judgment of industrial relations matters." 4.) Elimination of management and labor "agitators" who specialize in pre­senting biased wewpoints. 5.) More ade­quate communications and contacts between top level and lower level representatives in both management and labor. 6.) Increased educational activities carried on sinceriy and efficiently by both sides to instruct, in­form, and explain. 7.) A simplified, just, more understandable basic wage system. 8.) Increased non-financial rewards and recog­nition for workers. 
THE TENTH ANNIVERSARY of Ivotre Dame's REVIEW OF POLITICS was ob­served in October with a special omii­veisory edition. Under the editorship of Dr. Waldemar Gurian, the quarterly has gained ^vorld prominence. It 'was founded to "revive the Aristotelian con­ception of politics, a sovereign concep­tion that embraced everything, every interest, every event, every idea affect­ing the life of man in the community."

A NEW SCIENCE BUILDING to house the departments of physics, chemistry, and mathematics is in the planning stage at No­tre Dame. The announcement was made when the Universit>-'s Ad\'isory Council on Science and Engineering met here October 9, 10. 
The Rev. Philip S. Moore, C.S.G., dean of the Graduate School, told the Council that research is being carried on here in meat destructive parasites, plant growth, chemotherapeutic agents for cancer, anti­malarial drugs, atomic energy and radio­active substances, electronics, synthetic rub­bers and plastics, germ-free life, nutrition, instrumentation, aircraft structures, commu­nications, electro-plating, metals and alloys. 
Dean Karl Schoenherr, of the College of Engineering, told of proposed installation of a small towing tank, a water tunnel, and other fluid mechanics equipment for gradu­ate research.

Dr. Price came to Notre Dame in 1945 from the University of Illinois, where he had attained distinction in research in the field of high polymer chemistry. In 1946 he received the American Chem­ical Society's award to the outstanding chemist under the age of 35. 
During the war. Dr. Price devoted his time largely to direction of research projects for the National Defense Re­search Committee, the Chemical War­fare Service and the Committee on Med­ical Research. He was chairman of the Gibson Island Polymer Conference in 1947. and has served as constUtant to the Hercules Power Co., the Socony-Vacuum Oil Co., the Eli Lilly Co., and the Coca-Cola Co.

The Notre Dame Alumnus 

Ending a Chapter---Beginnins a Book^ 
Hany G. Hogan Addresses a Message to the Members of the Alumni Association as His Term of Office Nears Its Qose; The Message Has a Deep Significance for Notre Dame 
"On January 1, or shortly thereafter at the meeting of the Alumni Board, I shall terminate my two terms as president of the Alumni Association. It has been a tribute from my fellow alumni which I respect sin­cerely, and which I have tried to repay with the creation among alumni every^vhere of a program and a spirit and a record of achievement. 
"I feel that I am personally ending a chapter, a rich one for me. Many causes and many organizations have enlisted my volunteer time. But I have found in working for Notre Dame a spiritual motivation that has brought rewards beyond the usual sat­isfaction of civic or social group work. It has been one of the most convincing e\'i­dences of the permanence of Notre Dame's progress to find this feeling shared by hun­dreds of others with similar e-Npcrience. 
The Foundation 
"But if I am ending a chapter as presi­dent of the Alumni .Association, I feel that in the University of Notre Dame Foundation we have begun a book, ultimately a great 

book. 
"When I took office in January, 1947, the Alumni Association was pursuing a fine program of spiritual, fraternal, cultural, in­tellectual and social activity. And in the An­nual Alumni Fund it was building annually a substantial contribution to Notre Dame. 
"The temptation was to take this pre­pared formula as it stood, give it a blessing, and bask in the reflected glow. 
"But in going over the Notre Dame situa­tion with Father John Cavanaugh, I was appalled as a business man to discover the thin economic ice on which the University stood, and the economic barriers that stood in the way of its realization of opportuni­ties never before presented. Father Cava­naugh's own training had brought to him a quick analysis of his plight as president. 
"Resisting the temptation of the easy way. 


Science Jjulldln^ J^Unnea for fg^g 
Rev. John J. Cavanaugh, C.S.C., president of Notre Dame, re­cently announced that plans have been formulated for the construc­tion of a new Science Building in 1949. 
Drawings are being made for this building; the cost is estimated to be about $1,750,000. It will be placed adjoining the Chemistry Building, and is to provide extra space for Chemistry, as well as new facilities for the Departments of Physics and Mathematics. The building will be of simple construction, and as inexpensive as is pK)s­sible in a structiure designed for modern scientific research laboratories. 
The University's lack of facilities for the students and research staffs in physics, chemistry and mathematics is critical. Unrestricted contributions to Notre Dame, in 1946 and 1947, have built up a fimd of $350,000 which the University has designated as the beginning of the Science Building Fund. Because of the extreme urgency of this need. Father Cavanaugh has asked the alumni and the members of the Notre Dame Foundation to make an all-out effort to raise $1,400,000 by the end of 1949 so that construction may begin. 
The present need is so critical that the University is faced with the choice of constructing new facilities or of cutting the enrollment by about 1,000 students in order to make room for research to be carried on. 
I felt then, as I feel now, that this is an 

era for asking, not for basking. 
"Notre Dame's alumni, performing a very creditable job within their own ranks through the Alumni Fund, were numerically limited, and economically limited, at least to the extent indicated by Notre Dame's opportunities. 
"Notre Dame's Department of Public Re­lations, had begun the slow and arduous task of building up the information and the good will and the subsequent support of non-alumni friends. These friends were be­coming invaluable and generous parts of the Notre Dame program, but again the im­mediate demands of opportunity indicated that the program in effect would not supply the numbers nor the amounts. 
"The answer, in the Spring of 1947, was a united, coordinated and expanded agency for bringing Notre Dame's opportunities to the immediate attention of all alumni, all friends, and more friends—the University of Notre Dame Foundation. 

Response Most Encouraging 

"Response from alumni and friends to the new Foundation was immediate and beyond our actual cvpectations. 
"Governors were appointed for each of the States and other Notre Dame areas of importance. 
"These Governors, working with the Foundation Office and Father Cavanaugh, set about the joint task of creating policies, and at the same time creating the essential working base of the Foundation, the City Committees. The 222 cities with Chairmen, and the 184 City Committees, consisting of alumni and non-alumni friends, aie eloquent tribute to the zeal and the success of the Governors. 
"More than 1,000 alumni and friends are now working in a national (even interna­tional) organization as volunteer sales and public relations representatives for Notre Dame. 
"The national scope of Notre Dame's alumni and friends, the cosmopolitan nature of its enrollment demanded such an organi­zation. And such an organization is a guar­antee of ultimate success. 

Alumni Can Contribute Nothing Greater 
"No program of the alumni—and other phases of the Association's program continue —no other program, I repeat, could do as much for Notre Dame. Nor, in fact, could any other program do as much for Notre Dame alumni, who will grow in individual Volume 26, No. 6, Nov.-Dec, 1948 
HABRY G. HOGAN 

stature as the University grows, in its pres­tige and in its service. 
"From the success of Notre Dame, through the Foundation, will stem all of the great services in the field of spiritual, cul­tural and fraternal activities that will mul­tiply the benefits to alumni. 
Objectives 

"You know the objectives. Notre Dame is faced with adequate provision for 1,500 present students in excess of the 3,200 pre­war enrollment for whom the present facili­ties were already inadequate. 
"There are replacements needed in the near future for some of the grand old build­ings that have served well but long—Sorin, St. Edward's, Washington Hall, the Admin­istration Building, for example, totalling more than 250 years of usage. 
"Probably the most immediate objective is a Science Building to house adequately the great work in chemistry, physics and 
^ mathematics which have accumulated great academic records of achievement and pres­tige, which have acquired much invaluable equipment, and whose classrooms and lab­oratories are crowded with the young men of science who can give to tomorrow's Amer­ica the men who can control science, to­gether with the moral responsibility that can control men. 
"There are many other opportunities. You will be told about them in detail as the Foundation prograim progresses. 
Endowment Is Sadly Lacking 

"Because Notre Dame has never seen fit to accumulate wealth, but has poured back into student facilities the fruits of its labors and sacrifices over the years, the University finds itself in the paradoxical position of a leading university in prestige and program, but a virtual tail-ender in the field of com­parative endowments, with only $3,891,638, 
^ ranking it in the lowest tenth of the lead-
W ing 100 colleges and the universities in America. 
"That is primarily why the double-edged era of rising costs and rising opportunities has brought the University to the position of requiring substantial outside aid to achieve its objectives. 

The Future 

"American thinking, as reflected in its spiritual, its political, its economic, and its social life, is rapidly being forced to turn to the fundamentals which have been an integral part of Notre Dame education since Father Edward Sorin brought the great principles of Christian education to the site of the University in 1842, and found with joy that these coincided with the funda­mental principles of the great democracy of his adoption. 
"Everything points to the increasing ur­gency of the type of education which No­tre Dame gives. 
"If we succeed, as we can and as we shall, in letting the world know that this is the type of education which Notre Dame gives, then we should be confident that the proper support to preserve the institution giving it will be forthcoming. 
"Men are making great sacrifices for less worthy causes. 
"The work has been a source of satisfac­tion to me. Your cooperation, coming from so many, convinces me that you have shared in this satisfaction. 
"The University is deeply appreciative of the loyal, understanding response. 
"None of us will be satisfied unless this first chapter of the Foundation grows into the ultimate great booh that will be the story of Notre Dame."

President of the Alumni Association 

Chemists Meet 

Frank Pollnow of Vestal Laboratories, St. Louis, was in charge of arrangements as chemists associated with Notre Dame met in the Jefferson Hotel, St. Louis, on Sept. 7 
in conjunction with the fall convention of the American Chemical Society. 
Those present included Dr. Charles G. Price, Dr. Kenenth N. Campbell, James O. Knobloch, Joseph Pappalardo and Robert 
H. Schuler of the University of Nbtie Dame, as well as Harry L. Wundcrly, Mr. Pollnow, Daniel J. Pflaum, Michael J. O'Connor, Fred C. Weber, J., James F. Kerwin Jo­seph A. Batscha, Rev. Bertin Ending, O.S3., William S. Calcott and Rev. J. S. McGrath, C.S.C., the last named from the University of Portland, in Oregon. 
Alumni Directory Delayed 
The proposed "Alumni Directory, for which alumni have sent in information to a very encouraging degree, has been tempor­arily delayed by two major factors. 
One is the problem of finding a printer to consider the project within a desirable time limit (four or more months was the basic estimate). 
The second is the cost element. Seventy-five hundred (7500) copies of the proposed Directory would cost, estimates indicated, some three dollars ($3) each. It was not in the original plan to sell or to ask for spe­cial contributions to defray this cost. Indi­cations of the desire for the Directory from individual alimuii do not warrant optimism in this regard, even if the decision were altered. 
The information sent in on the Directory cards is not a waste of time, effort or ex­pense. 
Conununity lists are being prepared from these records, copies of which will be typed for the Notre Dame Club Presidents and for the Foundation City Committees. 
These lists, carrying not only names, ad­dresses and classes, but business classifica­tion appreciate the responses of alumni to uable addition to present lists. 
The Alumni Association and the Foimda­tion appreciate the response of alumni to the request for information, which arc a major contribution to our permanent records under any cireuimstances. And these records will provide a short cut to preparation if printing conditions, including cost, alter in the near future in our favor. 
Your support in 1948 has been a source of great encouragement and help. Because of the fine purpose to which Notre Dame is dedicated, we know that Our Lady and Her Beloved Son will bring you the real joys of Christmas and the prospects of a truly Happy, New Year. 
N-
President of the University Director of tfie Notre Dame Foundation

llie STUDENTS 
By MARTIN BROWNE 
The P. of D. Is Gone 
No student today undergoes the time-hallowed ordeal of answering a summons from the Prefect of Discipline. There is no Prefect of Discipline at Notre Dame! At the opening of the fall term. Rev. John J. Cav­anaugh, C.S.C, announced the abolishment of this post. In its place, a newly organized Department of Student Welfare has been set up, with Rev. Joseph K. Kchoe, C.S.C, former Prefect of Discipline, as its director. The physical, religious, recreational and general welfare of both undergraduate and graduate students are his concern. Assisting him. Rev. Joseph D. Barr>-, C.S.C, former Director of Student Acti^ties, will be charg­ed with enforcement of Universitj- regula­tions. {Everything can't go—but it does sound better!) 
Tied in with the above department, the Committee on Student Entertainment seeks to keep the boys so busy and diverted as to eliminate disciplinarj' problems. Besides Father Kehoe, it includes Prof. William J. Elsen of the Department of Speech, who will direct the University Theatre, Prof. Daniel S. Pedtke, Glee Club director. Prof. 
H. Lee Hope, Director of the Band, and Rev. Louis J. Thornton, C.S.C, registrar.

But Drama Comes Back 
Drama tradition at Notre Dame is to be revived with Professor Elsen's organization of the University Theatre. (Who of the latter-day turbulent t%venties can foi^t The Taming of the Shrew?) Prof. Cecil Birder's outstanding Gilbert and Sullivan produc­tions, and the student musicals written and produced by Ed Cashman, '48, have provid­ed cvcellcnt outlets for student actors with musical inclinations. But not much straight dra>Tna! The opening of the three-act com­edy The Milky Way, on Nov. 28, wiU re-Wve this art, and will presage the expected establishment of a Workshop Theatre.

The Notre Dame Glee Club joined voices with that of St. Mary's on Oct. 24 and 25 to present "The Annunciation Mystery," an interpretation of the Annunciation adapted for chorus and organ by Rev. John Gallagher, C.S.C, of the Department of Music, from a thirteenth century text. This is a concert version of the "chanted play," used in the Middle Ages to precede the Sacrifice of the

Mass on special occasions. Thus, even ven­erable Washington Hall witnessed something new, for nothing of the kind had ever be­fore been presented at the University. 
Alumni in and around Aurora and Elgin, 111., will be interested to learn that the Glee Club will give concerts in those cities on Dec. 10 and 11 respectively. 
Freshmen Band Together 
Continuing its practice of providing tune­ful and humorous entertainment between halves at the football games, Mr. Hope's band is this year presenting a series of Mu­sical Murals. The marching band is now comprised of 110 men. An innovation is the new and sprightly 50-piece freshman band, a separate unit, which plays at all pre­game pep rallies. 
Who can forget his student trips? Cer­tainly none of those who made this year's super-trip to Baltimore and Washington! In addition to the Navy game, it included t^vo nights at Washington's Annapolis Hotel and a gala Student Relief Dance at the Uline Arena. Members of the Washington-Mary­land-Virginia Club of Notre Dame, hosts for the dance, had previously set up an effi­cient date bureau which provided glamor­ous gals from Trinity, Georgetown Visita­tion and Dumbarton colleges. Larry Clin­ton and his band provided the music.

Bums, Jr., a senior in the University, has been elected national chairman of the drive, and heads committees from 151 member colleges of the National Federation of Catholic Col­lege Students. Girls at the Rock across the Dixie high­way were little disturbed by the flurry of Eastern competition raised by the Navy trip. They, as has been said before, are here. To advise Notre Dame students of the fact, a tea dance was held at St. Mary's on the first Sunday of the term, setting the stage for who-knows-what-romances which will lead to the "Marriages" column of this pub­lication. 
Victory Dances held in the Drill Hall after all football games, at home or away, have proved popular with both schools. 
Notre Dame and St. Mary's freshmen pic­nicked in Potawattomi Park on the after­noon of Oct. 16, with the boys as hosts. 
The old guard of the alumni, whose ro­mantic adventures were confined to the well-supervised lounge at St. Mary's and surreptitious dates in the Blackstone and Oliver balconies, will hail the new freedom which allows the boys and girls to stroll about the grounds at St. Mary's, dine in town, and even play softball together in Potawattomi Park! 
nft7 Dollar Nest Egg 
Vetville, Notre Dame's most human "hu­man interest" center, opened the year with great plans. A newly-formed Married Veter­an's Welfare Council, with Rev. Alfred Mendez, C.S.C., as moderator, will serve not only Vetville residents but also some 400 married vets living in South Bend and Mish­awaka. It seeks to "coordinate, enlarge and intensify all efforts toward the welfare of each veteran, his wife and his child." 
A Maternity Committee began a cam­paign to raise $5,000 so that the Council may make a payment of $50 to help toward hospital expenses of each veteran's wife (ap­proximately 100 babies are currently on the way). A recent survey disclosed that the av­erage vet has a monthly income of $236 through the government, his wife's earnings and his own. Average expenses are about $215 a month, leaving very little to assist Junior into the world. Anyone want to be fairy godfather for a G.I. baby?

The Union Forever 
The Student Union comes first with the students! This fall the Scholastic conducted a hall-to-hall poll to discover student opi­nion as to which building should be erected first under the new Notre Dame Founda­tion program. A huge majority showed pref­erence for the proposed Student Union. The need for space in which to entertain rela­tives, guests and friends was the leading rea­son for this choice. 
• • • " 
One of five Negro students presently en­

rolled at Notre Dame, and the first Negro ever to report for football practice here, is Aaron Dyson of Indianapolis, Miss. Dyson, a halfback, now under the tutelage of fresh­man coach Benny Sheridan, will perhaps be the second member of his race to win a major sports monogram at the University. Frazier Thompson, '47, of Philadelphia, a dash man on the track team, was the first. 
• • » 

Up they go! The Notre Dame unit of the Air ROTC soared into the wild blue yonder with an enrollment of four times its strength of last year, according to Capt. Thomas J. Hardy, adjutant. Inaugurated last year with a group of 65 trainees, it now boasts 270, of whom about 130 are freshmen. Thirty-five seniors, all veterans, will receive their reserve commissions as sec­ond lieutenants upon graduation. 
Chuck Wagon Style Alumni reunions are familiar. Reunions for students are something new, originated last August by the Texas Club of Notre Dame. A Roundup of students was held in Dallas, under the direction of John Brogan, of Tyler, president of the club, and John Francis Giles, Jr., of Dallas. Two dances, golfing, swimming and a chuck wagon barbecue were included in the gala week-end. Officers of the alumni club of Dallas entertained the visitors, at an open-house. A similar Roundup is planned for the week-end of the Southern Methodist University-Notre Dame basketball game, which will be played in Dallas on Dec. 30. « » •

The SWORD «/* SPIRIT 
By Paul Mallon, '23 
Time changes opinions sometimes. Events lure us from established cons'ictions. We like to think we are liberal and openminded, and we strive to keep pace with the march of progress by searching out the ever new circumstances the world presents and ad­justing our minds to them. Furthermore, the nature of the human in us is always seeking relaxation, ease—^and peace. We yearn for an end of toil and strife, and when involved in arduous ventures, we have an inclination to let up, or look forward to the moment when we can let up. 
All this may sound rather commonplace to you alumni of Notre Dame, but if either of these two natural courses is allowed to run its way in the current world struggle, we are lost—our alumni, our nation, our world. 
The critical hour for us at Pearl Harbor 
was the hour we did not consider, the hour 
before the. Japanese._struck, the hour in 
which we could have saved ourselves by 
preparation. This hour today is such an hour 
for an entire world. The critical danger 
confronting us is that in the ritsh and flow 
of our normal.lives, we \vill forget—as peo­
ple do—^the nature of the thing with which 
we are confronted. We may lapse into the 
belief This Thing is normal, and susceptible 
to the same human instincts we have, the 
love of agreement, the worship of progress, 
the respect of justice and the belief in peace. 
Facts grow cold, you know, unless constantly 
stimidated. Doctrines debilitate unless car­
ried as banners. 
To guard against a natural decline in our political.doctrines, it is essential that each one of us understand the nature of This Thing with which we are confronted. In a struggle to maintain the world intellect, we are daily meeting subtle lures and challenges. To meet these we must never for a moment lay down the Sword of the Spirit, which defends true knowledge of the character of the lure and challenge. 
What I mean can be made plain by an in­cident in the final days of the first Truman administration. The President himself, with all his knowledge of the subject, thought it woidd be an excellent campaign gesture for him to send .the chief justice of the United States Supreme Court to Moscow to show— as he said—our "seriousness and sincerity" in favor of an understanding to restore order in Berlin. In the ordinary course of nonnal negotiations between nations, this would seem harmless—a move of simple earnest­ness, the dispatch of our chief justice to seek fair justice. What danger could there be in that? 
To our alumni, informed in various de­grees concerning the brutal efforts of the Russians to defeat all contrary interests in Berlin, the project could have sounded in­nocent. The Democrats among us could eas­ily, and no doubt did, deduce that the Republicans clamored about the matter for 
PATH. MALLON 
partisan political effect (which they no doubt had in mind) and thus some of us may have been misled as to the unsound nature of the venture. 
Mr. Truman's plan was innocent in but one respect. It was completely innocent of an understanding of Russianism, commun­ism or whatever This Thing may properly be designated. 
The character of This Thing may, per­haps,' be understood by an incident i^c h occurred in a little place where I spent some time this summer. I have recently made a longer study of its motivating power but this one incident conveys some of the The Notre Dcnne Alumnus 

accurate implications of what lies so heavily against us. In this village was a fisherman who went off alone in his boat each day to tong clams from the bottom of the bay. When a friend of mine missed a new five pound navy anchor which had been spliced to his rowboat, he asked around among the villagers. One told him: "You know that fisherman who is always working down in the cove. His name is Charles. Go ask Charles." My friend could pry no explanation from his advisor. His personal inclination was to seek out the police. Yet, perhaps the clam tonger had seen children cut away the anchor while working on his boat. My friend went down to the cove and found Charles assiduously cleaning his craft. 
"Charles," he called. "Have you seen my new anchor?" 
His ears jumped in surprise when Charles immediately called back: "Certainly I think I have it right here somewhere. Let's take a look." 
The fisherman, pleasantly, opened the floor boards at the bottom of his boat and started rummaging among an assortment of new anchors, ropes and boat accessories which had no useful place on his small mo­torboat. Handling them as a merchant dis­plays his wares, he brought forth one and said: "I think this is yours." "It was—new rope and all, even the portion which had been cut. 
My friend did not know exactly how to handle this situation. He was not sure what could best be said under the circumstances. So he said: "Why, thank you Charles, thank you very much," and went off to attach his anchor back to his rowboat. 
Inquir)- among the villagers developed a further knowledge of the working philoso­phy of Charles. One explained: "If he sees anything he likes, he just takes it." He need have no normal motive, they said, such as a use for the stolen article or a desire to enrich himself. He was completely free of normal reactions in this respect. He had no inhibitions whatever against thievery-. When­ever anything was missing around the cove, the villagers never reported the loss to the single village constable, but first asked Charles, and usually, from the bottom of his boat, he brought forth proudly the miss­ing article. 
This Thing with which we are confronted in the world is, in moral essence, an inter­national Charles. It would be considered idiotic if the village had appointed a Select­man to see Charles and impress him with the "seriousness and sincerity" of the com­munity. In treating with Russia we have found the summoning of an international court, committee or police force is general­ly useless. We have found serious discussion of the nature of conditions leads to involve­ment in degenerating arguments and finally achieves only a sense of futility in us. We have found none of the normal reactions from the normal use of diplomatic measures. Efforts to settle are interpreted by Russia as designs to conquer her, and therefore do not settle, but antagonize. Our strivings for disarmament, and for atomic control, are 
Volume 26, No. 6, Nov.-Dec, 1948 
treated by the Russians as a diplomatic game to be frustrated. Disarmament becomes in the relentless view of the Kremlin a prob­lem of how to disarm us, while Stalin may achieve more and more armament unknown to us behind his Iron Curtain. 
We have learned we are dealing with an adversary which lacks nearly all our normal concepts. In this experience, we are rapidly approaching, if we have not already passed, an inevitable conclusion that moral suasion has no use. 
Does this mean war must be fought? It should not. I think war would be a stupid conclusion of a difficult situation, like that of a novelist who has his hero commit sui­cide at the end of the book because he, the novelist, cannot solve the difficulties other­wise. War would be a confession of our diplomatic ineptitude. To me an American War against the Russians would be almost as senseless as if the villagers in my summer seaport town had declared war on the type­that-is-Charles, and armed themselves with sticks and stones to kill him. 
Russia is a condition, a situation. It must be met. We must meet it effectively in ways which are more efficient for us. Our current ways and course are not necessarily, per­manently to be considered the best ways. We are spending a third or more of our entire national budget for armament—$12 to $15 billions annually. We have foolishly blundered into a condition in the German peace whereby we must spend needed mil­lions needlessly for planes to feed and warm our portion of Berlin by air. In meeting the Russians along these lines, we are squander­ing our substance, weakening ourselves and serving their ultimate purposes. In our whole policy, we have permitted Russia too much freedom to contrive ways for weaken­ing our capitalism. 
The struggle in its current phase is but in a temporar}', stalemated and perhaps ad­\-anced stage, I have no doubt history will eventually record. Much can and must be done with it by us, now and every day. A new Truman administration doubtless will seek to effect new measures to handle This Thing more effectively. Surely we must have new and constantly more efficient lead­ership, ever seeking new and better methods but not forgetful of the sad experience through which we have moved to emerge from propaganda delusions to our present understanding. 
Notre Dame alumni cannot possibly keep up with events as they march. We can only pick up newspapers occasionally at our lei­sure, or listen to the radio when we have opportunit>". By such hop-skip-and-jump reading and learning, we may not hope to acquire the knowledge of experts. But we will not be deluded if we keep our minds clear in the knowledge of the true nature of This Thing, and defend this basic under­standing with the Sword of the Spirit against intrusions which will daily be attempted upon our intellect. Let this understanding be Our guide-measure, ^vithout cessation, with­out deviation. 
This is essential.

Generation Club Begins Activities for 1948-49 
HOW MANY FACES LOOK 
By MURRAY POWERS, '51 

Ever>'thing from socials to lectures is on the calendar of the Generation Club for the rest of the schoolyear. One of the foremost plans is the decorating of the Huddle. 
(The Generation Club, you'll recall, is composed of the sons or grandsons of alum­ni. George Witteried, Jr., is the president and Rev. Eugene Burke, C.S.C, is the fac­ulty advisor) 
The Huddle was formerly Brother Leo­pold's candy store. In it one can buy most anything from sodas, cones and cokes to cigarettes, candy and soap. Shelves line the blank walls to hold the books and coffees of the ever-present throngs of students. 
It was the blank walls that first prompted the redecorating idea. The aim now is to make it really a huddle, and not just a re­freshment stand. College spirit and tradition will reign, with pennants and pictures on the walls. The pictures will be of the new and the old Notre Dame, the teams and scenes of the past and the present. They will be procured from old collections on the campus and from various alumni. Also there will be posted the scores of various impor­tant athletic contests. 
This renovating is under the supervision

On the social side, the club plans to have a dinner party sometime in February. The dinner will be for members of the club and girls of St. Mar>''s College. It is hoped that the men will put on a skit for entertain­ment. Also there will be dancing and group singing. 
Tentatively planned also are lectures in parliamentary procedure for the club and addresses by prominent alumni to the stu­dent body. 
A tutor bureau is contemplated also so that those needing academic help and those willing to give it could readily join forces. 

Also for the future is a scholarship fimd. This fund would be for the needy sons of alumni, to help them complete their educa­tion. The administration of such a fund would be in the hands of three Notre Dame graduates. 

Low Group Progresses 
With the report of the nominating com­mittee, the organizational plans for the elec­tion and meeting of the first board of direc­tors of the Notre Dame Law Association have been completed. A copy of the Consti­tution, a ballot, and an explanatory letter will be mailed to all Notre Dame men who are listed as lawyers in the records avail­able at the University. The first meeting of the board will be held on Dec. 10 and 11, coinciding with the proceedings of the Sec­ond Natural Law Institute. The agenda will include the election of officers, adoption of by-laws to implement the general provisions of the constitution, and establishment of a central office from which to effectuate the purposes for which the Association was or­ganized. Pending the initial meeting, in­quiries may be directed to the Notre Dame Law Association, Box 1393, Notre Dame, Ind. 
Notre Dame Book Store Adds Modem Note 
Yes, that's what we said: this is the Notre Dame Book Store of 1948. Bub your eyes and look again. 
As Notre Dame students of the early 1920s, among others, can testify, things are not what they used to be on the campus. 
Take the Book Store. Wasn't it only yes­terday that Brother Prosper and his corps of student helpers were dealing out books and stationerj' from a tiny room in the Main Building, first floor back? Space there was so restricted that the room had a "mez­zanine," which meant a sort of catwalk eight feet up or so where the nimble-footed employes, having ascended a ladder, im­periled their lives in search of an elusive physics text. 
In 1932, the Book Store forsook its hum­ble quarters in the Main Building and mov­ed to Badin (formerly St. Joseph's) Hall to take over space once given to the Badin "rec room" on the ground floor, south, of the building. There, under the expert man­agement of Brother Meinrad, C.S.C., and Brother Conan, C.S.C., his assistant, the store grew fast, seeing one of its busiest pe­riods in World War II when thousands of naval trainees crowded the campus. Many lines of merchandise were added, especially Notre Dame jewelry, the handling of which was transferred in 1942 from the Comp­trollers' Office to the Book Store. 
But still the Book Store was cramped, especially with the expanded student body following the war. So a year ago. Brother Conan, now manager of the store took over space on the north side of Badin, ground floor, and concentrated there the text book division of the Book Store. (This, as alum­ni of the 1920s will recall, was the scene of the memorable O. A. Clark cafeteria). 
With the start of Christmas vacation in 1947, renovators attacked the south divi­sion of the Book Store and on Feb. 1, 1948, it reappeared as the modem store pictured above, with distinctive merchandise, display

At Military Conference 
Rev. John H. Murphy, C.S.C., vice-presi­dent, was named to a panel to participate in a ten-day military orientation conference from Nov. 8 to 18 sponsored by the Depart­ment of Defense. 
Leaders in industrial, business and pro­fessional fields were invited to the confer­ence, held to acquaint the group with cur­rent activities and problems of the armed forces. Father Murphy and other members of the pane] visited military baess including Fort Benning, Ga., Pensacola, Fla., Naval Base, and the Eglin Air Base at Valparaiso, Fla. 




Notre Dame Books 
FIRST CITIZEN. By Richard Sullivan, '30. New York: Henry Holt and Co. $3.00 
Review by {Rev.) Leo L. Ward, C.S.C. 
The publication of another novel by Rich­ard Sullivan, associate professor in tht De­partment of English at Notre Dame, will be interesting news to many readers of the ALUMNUS. It is not surprising to find that First Citizen, Mr. Sullivan's fourth novel, maintains the high level of craftmanship which characterized his earlier stories, while, at the same time, it achieves a stronger dramatic quality and a richer, deeper meaning. 
The story turns around the career of Kingsley Bond, "first citizen" of the small Midwestern city of Baysweep, and the influ­ence of his career on his wife Una and his daughter Elizabeth. Kingsley Bond is con­sumed by lust for money and position. After years of disappointment, he suddenly gains the money by unscrupulously securing a very high insurance indemnit>- from the death of his eldest daughter Monica in an automobile accident; and, with the money, by shrewd and callous bargaining, he gradually gains the position and influence which wealth can bring. Knowing how he has exploited the death of Monica, and feeling that she is somehow involved, his wife Una is gradually driven to a condition of insane fear, while his daughter Elizabeth is brought to drunk­en and reckless despair, only to be saved from herself, and from the contamination of her father's guilt, by Pete Morrissey, the one solid rock of sense and understand­ing in the chaos of doubt and emotion into which Elizabeth has been flung. The story actually relates only the events of one night and one morning in the lives of the char­acters, but the writer manages to unfold, by a skillful use of flashbacks which propel rather than retard the story, the whole life of each character. The narrative method is admirably suited to Mr. Sullivan's purpose. While it enables him to achieve a unity and totality of eff'cct which, as one reviewer has remarked, is almost as simple and stark as Greek tragedy, it also permits the author to explore the complexity of his characters fully, and to reveal the whole range of their emotional experience. The result is a story filled with strong dramatic tension. One re­viewer has called it a novel of pain and pas­sion. It is this and more: a novel in which inner spiritual suH'ering is rendered fearless­ly and relentlessly, but with tender compas­sion, and, finally, with Christian meaning. 
The meaning is implicit in Una's fright­ful action as she transfixes her o^vn hand with hammer and nail in a scene of high passionate drama; and the meaning is also revealed in the figure of Pete Morrissey, whose clear common sense, braced and en­lightened by his religious faith, helps Eliza­beth to accept in the end a world in which human nature seems so often heir to tragic folly. In the lives of Una and Elizabeth par­ticularly, and, with fearful and devastating 
RICHARD SULLIVAN 

irony, even in the life of Kingsley Bond as well, the common birthmark of the Cross is clear. The Crucifixion is both a shadow and a light falling across all the pages of the novel. The author faces the fact of human suH'ering frankly, with no sentimental or pious compromises, but the sufl'ering is nei­ther futile nor blind, for in the end.it has a clear overtone of Christian meaning. Never didactic or obtrusive, the meaning is yet clear, a light against which the characters emerge in sharp relief. First Citizen is grim and terrible, but also tender and beautiful, because it is a novel that never could have been written in a world that had not known the passion and charity of Christ.

Lecfures Available 

Dr. Glenn T. Seaborg of the University of California, one of the world's foremost nuclear chemists, delivered in the fall of 1947 the Nieuwland Memorial Lectures at Notre Dame. These notable lectures have now been gathered in a booklet entitled "The Transuranium Elements and Nuclear Energy," published by Notre Dame and available through the Chemistry Depart­ment, Notre Dame, Ind., at a cost of one dollar a copy. 
Preference Preferred 
Alumni, it appears, are very definitely in favor of giving preference in the matter of purchasing football tickets to those who con­tribute to the Annual Alumni Fund. 
Such is the verdict resulting from the "ballot" enclosed with the September-October ALUMUNS. 
There were, up to Nov. 5, answers total­ing 1,481 with regard to football ticket pref­erence. Of this number, 471 believed that "some" preference should be given to con­tributors, while 824 voted specifically in favor of giving preference to those who had contributed in the previous calendar year. Only 186 felt that no preference at all should be given to contributors. 
The whole matter will be considered by the Board of Directors of the Alumni Asso­ciation at its next meeting.

ATHLETICS • • 
By RAYMOND J. DONOVAN. '42, Assistant Director oi Public Inionnation 

Football 

Heading down the home stretch toward the conclusion of the 1948 football season. Coach Frank Leahy's Notre Dame football team was faced with overwhelming odds in its attempt to retain the national champion­ship for the third consecutive year. 
The undefeated Irish eleven, \vith six con­secutive 1948 victories to its credit as this was written, had run the unbeaten string chalked up by Notre Dame gridders to 24— but the Leahymen were ranked No. 2 in the nation by the Associated Press- poll. 
Never below the third spot in the poll during the first half of the season, Notre Dame nevertheless held first place only once. After the opening week of the rankings, in which the Irish took over in first place just where they left off at the end of the 1947 campaign, they dropped to second place be­hind North Carolina and the following week to third place behind the Tarheels and Michigan. At this writing, Notre Dame had jumped back into second place, but the odds of shaking the Wolverines from first place were pretty formidable. 
Probably the greatest apparent difference between the 1947 Notre Dame national championship eleven and this year's aspir­ing team is that last year's squad gave the impression of never ha%-ing to turn on their full power to sweep to an unbeaten cam­paign. The 1948 Irish, minus some of the reserve power utilized last year have had to go "all out" on several occasions to avoid defeat. 
And Coach Leahy's defending champions didn't have to wait very long before they had to cut loose with everything they had. In the season opener against Purdue, hailed in pre-season ballyhoo as the "game of the year," the Notre Dame team found them­selves behind for the first time since 1945, only a second half spurt and a Boilermaker rally that fell only one point short kept the Irish unscathed to the tune of 28 to 27 in one of the most thrilling games ever played in the Notre Dame Stadium. 

A Pittsburgh team which only the week before had given highly touted Southern Methodist a terrific battle for three quarters before bowing 33 to 14, had little to offer against the Irish and Notre Dame made it two in a row this year and 20 straight over three season by trouncing the Panthers, 40 to 0. 
Michigan State's Spartans provided the first . opportunity for comparing scores against common opponents of both Notre Dame and Michigan. The Wolverines, in the season ogener, had been forced to come back from a 7-7 halftime deadlock to edge past the State eleven, 13 to 7. Featuring a rug­ged line that outcharged the. Irish during the first half, the Spartans once again ex­tended the Leahymen before bowing, 26 to 7, in a second half Notre Dame drive. 
Nebraska's Comhuskers submitted with no major objections, but not before they had pushed across two touchdowns, 44 to 13. And the Irish were forced to extend them­selves against Dr. Eddie Anderson's Iowa Hawkeyes. The Hawks held Notre Dame to a 6 to 6 deadlock in the first quarter, but the Irish picked up touchdowns in each suc­ceeding quarter to win, 27 to 12, in a game that found Notre Dame completing only one of eight passes, and that for a loss of seven yards. 
Coach Leahy's squad had little difficulty in disposing of a hapless Navy team that is in the midst of one of its most disastrous seasons, 41 to 7, for its twenty-fourth straight non-defeat game. 
But the Irish head down the home stretch without a blemish on their record, and they do this in spite of a siege of injuries that has hampered them throughout the season, and in spite of the fact that capable re­placements had to be found for such 1947 stalwarts as quarterback Johnny Lujack, tackles George Connor and Ziggy Czarob­ski and a host of others. 
Along the line Coach Leahy has depended consistently for a first team on Jim Martin and Leon Hart at ends. Jack Fallon and Ralph McGehee at tackles, Capt. Bill Fisch­er and Marty Wendell at guards, and Bill Walsh at center. 
It is difficult to pin the backfield down to any four men. Frank Tripucka definitely has been the No. 1 quarterback, but Bob Williams also has seen considerable action. At left half, Terry Brennan has had a tough time with an injured knee and has been spelled on occasion by Bill Gay and by Coy McGee until the latter broke his leg in prac­tice. Ernie Zalejski finally is rounding into shape after an injured leg. Emil Sitko con­tinues to hold down the right half post, but the work of Lancaster Smith and Jack Lan­dry has been outstanding. Fullbacks John Panelli and Mike Swistowicz both have been 
Orocko (leit) lacks that field goal in the Purdue game. At right Head Freshman Coodi Benny Slieridan tells Iiis boys how it's done. 
hampered off and on with injuries, and Frank Spaniel has stepped in to give credit­able performances. 
. So, the situation boils down to a Notre Dame team that is seeking to give the Irish a third straight national championship—a team that bears up well in the footsteps of illustrious predecessors—trying to knock a team out of the national lead, a team not on the Notre Dame schedule. 
Basketball 

Bolstered by the return of last season's entire starting lineup, Coach Ed Krause has opened practice for one of the toughest schedules in the history of basketball at No­tre Dame. 
The Irish quintet, with none of the tradi­tional "breather" opponents, open their 1948-49 card against Illinois on Dec. 8 in the Notre Dame Gym. Before the first of the year, the Krausemen must face five op­ponents from the tough Big Nine conference —and that's only the beginning. 
Features of the 24 game schedule will be trips to both the east and west coasts. Dur­ing the Christmas holidays the Irish will head west to meet Southern Methodist in Dallas, Tex., and St. Mary's in San Fran­cisco. Later in the season, Coach Krause will lead his quintet to the eastern seaboard, where the Notre Dame cagers will meet Ca­nisius in Buffalo, N. Y., and New York Uni­versity in Madison Square Garden. 
In addition to these contests, the Irish also will meet such basketball powerhouses as Northwestern, Wisconsin, Purdue, Indi­ana, DePaul, Butler, Denver, • Michigan State, Marquette, St. Louis, Pennsylvania Navy and Kentucky. 
Coach Krause was greeted by eight mon­ogram winners from last year's squad when practice opened late in October. Only John (Buster) Hiller and John Goonen were lost by graduation, but another major loss was suffered by a transfer. Vince Boryla, all-time scoring record holder at Notre Dame and 1948 Olympic hoop star, transferred to Den­ver University, instead of returning to No­tre Dame as had been anticipated. 
The potential starting lineup for the Irish five looms as the same one that carried the burden last year, when Notre Dame won 17 while losing 7, and included among its victims such highly-touted teams as Ken­tucky and New York University. 
Leo Barnhorst, leading scorer last year and husky rebound expert, likely will team with speedy Jim O'Halloran at the forward posts. John Brennan, leading scorer two years ago, who was out of action most of last year with a broken arm, is back at cen­ter. The starting guard positions undoubt­edly will be manned by defensive stalwart Paul Gordon and ambidextrous dribbler and ball-handler extraordinary Kevin O'Shea. 
Top flight reserves at this writing figure to include center John Foley, who took over the regular center berth last year while Brennan was injured, and forwards Dick Kluck and Frank Kaufmann. Likely look­ing sophomore prospects include Neal Fich-The complete schedule follows:

Cross-Counfr/ 

Four reliable veterans and a trio of prom­ising newcomers are shouldering the burden of Coach Elvin R. (Doc) Handy's 1948 Notre Dame cross country team. 
Veterans around whom the Irish harriers have been built include Jim Murphy, Bill Leonard, Jim Kittell, and Lou Tracy. New­comers who have bolstered Coach Handy's squad to date are Tony DaDamio, Lou Lepry and Gerry Johnson. 
The Irish cross-country runners opened the season by overwhelming a strong Wyo­ming team, but Wisconsin's Don Gehrmann and his mates proved too much of a hurdle and the Badgers handed the Handymen their initial loss. Notre Dame came back to edge past Iowa in its final duel meet of the season. 
Remaining on the Irish card are Navy and Villanova in a triangular meet at Bal­timore, Md., the Indiana state meet and the Central Collegiate meet, both at Notre Dame, and the national cross country run at Michigan State. 
CENTER BOX WALSH 
Fencing 

For the past several weeks. Herb Mel­ton's swordsmen have been working out for the 1948-49 fencing season. With thirty men out for the vanity and with five home matches, the squad b expected to attract much attention on the Irish campus. 
Although the schedule hasn't been approv­ed yet, N. D. is slated to play host to Chi­cago, Illinois, Northwestern, Ohio State and Illinois Tech. Teams like these will malce it the best home schedule since the sport was resumed here in 1946. 
Twrelve lettermen return including ex-captain Lou Bums, Jerry Lubin, Colin Mac­Donald and Mike DiCicco in the foils; Bob Schlosser, Bob Bloser, Ralph Witucki, and Tom Roncy in the sabre; and Ralph Dixon, John Vincent, Jerry Dobyns and Jim Jan­sen in the epee.

For those who missed the Boston Club news in the September-October 1948 issue of the ALUMNUS, we wish to advise that, for reasons best known to the editor and/or printer, it appeared under the heading of "Rhode Island and Boston.'* [The print­er's fault, naturally. We're sorry—Ed.] 
The next meeting will be held in November* The Club is planning to fall in line with the request of the Alumni Association to install new ofHcers on Universal Notre Dame night. For this reason, the annual election will be postponed until after the first of the year. A nominating committee will be appointed at the November meeting. 
We hated to use the "Go West, voung man" cliche, but TOM QUALTERS, '28, did just that. He is now assistant director of industrial relations for the Kaiser Steel Corporation at Fontana, Calif. Vice President FRANK H. REILLY and FRANCIS 
H. GUINDON are assisting President JOHN V. MORAN in the Greater Boston Community Fund Campaign.

BILL MIDDENDORF 

Eastern Indiana 
BILL CRAIG, '29, upon being elected president of our club, promised a stag golf tourney and din­ner sometime during 1948. This meeting was held Sept. 16 at the Delaware Country Club, near Mun­cie. About 50 N. D. men and friends of N. D. were present. 
The younger and hardier members teed off at 
1:30 for a blind bogey golf tourney, while we more sedentary oldsters retired to the club house for an afternoon of less strenuous pastime. After the scores were all posted and the kibitzing finished we joined together lor a very delightful meal. During the meal it was determined that BILL CRAIG won the golf trophy. To relieve his embarrassment, the prize was consumed on the spot. Some football neu-srecls were shown and there were discussions relative to club affairs.

Indianapolis 
JOHN REYNOLDS, local Cathedral High School graduate, was recently auarded the Notre Dame scholarship sponsored by the club. This annnal award is open to high school graduates of all the Indianapolis schools, based on competitive examina­tions submitted to and graded by the College En­trance Examination Board, Princeton, N. J. 
PAT FISHER, executive secretary of the club and a member of the Scholarship Award Committee, has announced that the funds for this award are es­tablished through the proceeds of special football trains to the \'arious games each fall. 
The first special to the Purdue game proved to be a big success, with more than five hundred people aboard. Specials will also be run to the Navy, Northwestern and Southern California games.

The Notre Dame Alumnus 

Notre Dame Club of Kentucky Observes Its 25th Anniversary 
Members of the Kentucky 
REV. JOHN J. CAVANAUGH, C.S.C, president of the Universit)-, gave the princi­pal address as the Kentucky Club obser\-ed its 25th anniversary with a dinner in the Brown Hotel, LouisWUe, on Sept. 20. REV. FRANCIS P. CWANAUGH, C.S.C, dean of the College of .\rts and Letters, and JAMES E. ARMSTRONG, alumni secre­tary, were also present from the University. 

Sixty-three of the 67 dues-paying members of the club were among the 361 at the din­ner. The following charter members were present: FRANK H. BRESLIN, FR.'MSIK B. BLOEMER, JOHN P. D.\NT, W. H. MILLS, CORNELIUS J. PFEIFFER and EDWARD H. PFEIFFER. 
The guests (see accompanying picture) included some of the most notable figures in Kentucky life. They were presented by JOSEPH F. DONALDSON, '23, toastmas­ter. JAMES EMMETT COSTELLO, '19, was chairman of the dinner and its marked success was due in large measure to his en­thusiasm, energy—and weeks of work. 
Father John Cavanaugh told the dinner that the spirit of Notre Dame comes from the family life of the students on the cam­pus and the University's insistence on the place of God in education. And he pointed out to the Kentuckians that their state had long had close ties with Notre Dame. Father Stephen Badin, first priest ordained in the United States, who is buried at Notre Dame, was a pioneer missionary in Ohio and Kentucky, he pointed out. 
"Father Badin purchased the land on which Notre Dame stands. He was, in a real sense, the pre-founder of Notre Dame," Father Cavanaugh declared. 
Father Cavanaugh also paid tribute to three Kentuckians who have played a con­spicuous part in Notre Dame life. The late FATHER LEONARD CARRICO, C.S.C, 
 Club and their guests at the club's 25lh anniretsary dinner Sept. 20*

The club ran an 11-car special train to the Purdue game, carr>-ing a sellout crowd of 350, and the special train to the North­western game was a sellout early in October. On the latter train 50 orphans will go along as guests of the club. Food and refreshments for them will be provided by local bene­factors. 
The club bowling season started in Sep­tember with 20 members participating on four teams. This promises to be the club's best bowling season since the war. 
Mohawk Valley 
PROF. JOHN H. SHEEHAN, former resident of Utica, N. Y., now the head of the Department of 
Economics at Notre Dame, was the chief speaker as the club on Sept. 9 sponsored a dinner in the Utica Hotel, Utica. An eight-point program to help achieve industrial peace between labor and man­agement was presented by Professor Shcehan. 
REV. ROBERT H. SWEENEY, C.S.C, executive assistant to the president, was another campus rep­resentative and speaker. Father Sweeney discussed Not--e Dame's philosophy of education. 
One hundred forty-five persons, including many representatives of management and labor and a large quota of present Notre Dame students, were present. Especially welcome were a group of Professor Shee­han's friends from the American Express Co., where he was formerly employed. DON FULLEKI was chairman of the dinner, assisted by FRANK DON­.ALTY, CHARLIE HITZELBERGER, BOB LACK­EY, VINCE FLETCHER, MIKE McGUIRL, JR., EARL HACKETT, ED SWEENEY and LOUIS CLARK. Mike McGuirl did a splendid job of in­teresting the present Notre Dame students. 
Frank Donalty presided in the absence of DR. FRANK MARINO, club president, who was called away from the dinner. City Judge Walsh was the £; toastmaster. ^ 
New Jersey 
Our annual Freshmen Welcome early in September was a marked success. Seventeen freshmen were there, along with 14 fathers and many upperdassmen and alumni, and they all saw and liked the campus film in sound and color from the University. JO­SEPH M. BYRNE and RAY GEIGER welcomed the new Notre Dame students. HANK BORDA was chairman of the afliair and TOM HOGAN was vice chairman. 
TOM FARRELL and BILL MAHON as chairman and vice chairman, respectively, ran the Football Rally sponsored by the club on Oct. 25 at the New­ark Athletic Club. The football film from the Uni­versity, "The Highlights of the 1947 Football Sea­son,'* was shown. 
The club ran a special train to the Navy game m Baltimore on Oct. 30 with DAN O'NEILL and PETE QUINN in charge.

Panama 
LORENZO RAMAGOSA was host on Oct. 7 at a luncheon at the Panama Golf Club in honor of REV. WILLIAM F. CUNNINGHAM, C.S.C., of the University. Father Cunningham was returning from La Paz, Bolivia, where he served as official representative for the National Catholic Education A^odation at the recent meeting of the Catholic Inter-American Education Conference. Members and guests at the luncheon included, Iiesides Father Cunningham, JOSE CARDENAS, RAMON RAMO­GOSA, PABLO DURAN, WILLIAM SHERIDAN, LEO J. KRZIZA and LORENZO RAMOGOSA. 
The club had a dinner at the Union Club on Sept. I in honor of JOSE CARDENAS and JACK SWAIN. The former was leaving for Italy to con­tinue his architectural studies in city planning and Mr. Swain was returning to Notre Dame to con­tinue work toward his master's degree.

Portland 
The summer was marked by a showing of the "Football Highlights of 1947," with 25 dub members present. Early in October the officers met at the University of Portland to map out the dub program for remainder of the year. Definitely set were the annual Communion-breakfast on Dec. 5, to be held at the University- of Portland, and Universal Notre Dame Night on April 25. A business meeting is on the program for a date in the spring still to be determined. 
Officers for the year are ED SANDSTROM, pres­ident; DORWIN PALMER, vice-president; FRED CUNNINGHAM, secretary-treasurer. Director are DOMINIC CALLICRATE, ROBERT MOLIN and PETE SANDROCK. 
St. Louis 
As this is written, we are in the midst of our annual Scholarship Fund Drive. Enthusiasm is run­ning high under the able leadership of JACK GRIF­FIN, *39, and it is expected that this will be our best year to date. 
BOB HELLRUNG, '30, gave a ver>- interesting and informative talk at a recent meeting when a welcome was given to new students and their dads. 
Our dub will be well represented at the coming Northwestern game as this is our only football trip scheduled this year. Club President FRED McNEILL, '36, reports that the several hundred resen'ations were taken up immediately.

% Washington^ D. C. 
The September meeting was attended by about 100 members. All prospective freshmen from the Washington area were invited to meet each other and some of the older graduates, prior to leaving for school. All members of the campus club still in town were also invited. 
The dub presented a supply of stationery to the Washington, Mar^-land and Virginia campus dub. It was accepted by GUY PERENICK, president of the dub. 
PAUL TULLY outlined plans for the Navy Game Rally held Oct. 29, at the Washington Hotel. A movie of the highlights of the 1947 football games was shown. At the October meeting TOM O'REIL­LY was appointed chairman of Universal Notre Dame Communion Breakfast to be held Dec. 5. 
Dr. Leo Stock, historian and philosopher, gave an interesting address on "An Historical Approach to the Problems of Church and State." Dr. Stock is a consultant in the Division of Hbtorical Research in the Carnegie Institute in Washington and a pro­fessor of History at Trinity College. A past president of the American Catholic Historical Association, he is the author of "Consular Relations between the United States and the Papal States." 
JOSEPH P. McNAMARA, '29, after some time in this area, left us for Bridgeport, Conn.

Biff RoocA Rvtirt 
WnUAM G. BOACH 

Fifty-five years as a barber. Bill Roach felt, was justification enough for his recent retirement as manager of the Notre Dame barber shop. For more than a quarter of a^ century. Bill had been at Notre Dame—one of the school's best known people. 
Bill—officially, William G.—^was bom in Akron, O., and came to Notre Dame in 1921. In 1923, he was named manager of the barber shop, in the days when it was on the ground floor of Washington Hall. In 1932, the shop moved to its present quarters in Badin Hall. The staff, which was made up of three barbers in 1923 and now num­bers six, comprised its greatest number dur­ing the war years, when eight barbers were kept busy trimming the heads of the Navy and the Marine trainees, the civilian stu­dents and the Commimity members. 
Bill and his wife reside at 721 Corby Blvd. in South Bend. His daughter, Jose­phine, of Xx)uisville, a St. Mary's graduate, is married to Norman L. Johnson, a Notre Dame graduate of 1936. 
Presidents of the University, famous names among the students, scared freshmen, —^Bill worked on them all during his days on the campus. But who does he consider his most famous ciutomer? 
That one was easy for him. His quick answer: Knute K. Rockne. Leonard McC!ollum, for years a barber in the Notre Dame shop, is the new manager.

Deaths 
REV. JOHN G. MCGINN, G.S.G., '06, 69 years old, first full-time secretary of the Alumni Association, died on Sept. 14 in the Gommunity Infirmary at Notre Dame after a long illness. 
One of the most widely known and res­pected of the Congregation's priests. Father McGinn achieved national prominence both as a professor of sociology and as an alumni administrator and the principal executive in Notre Dame's first endo\vment campaign, in the early 1920's. 
Ordained in 1910, Father McGinn re­ceived his S.T.L. from Catholic University, Washington, D. C , in the same year. In 1921 he was appointed the first fiiU-time secretary of the Notre Dame Alumni Associ­ation and in 1922 was instrumental in founding the ALUMNUS. He retired because of illness three years ago. 
Surviving him are three sisters and two brothers, all of Providence, R. I., where Father McGinn was bom on Sept. 10, 1879. 
Rev. Eugene Burke, C.S.C., was celebrant of the funeral Mass for Father McGinn on Sept. 18 in Sacred Heart Church, Notre Dame. Rev. Dean O'Donnell, C.S.C, was deacon and Rev. Augustine Reverado, C.S.C, sub-deacon. Burial was in the Com­munity Cemetery. 
[Father McGinn's death takes away a memorable figure, a man to whom in large measure the present stature of the Alimmi Association is due, a teacher and an execu­tive of rare ability. The ALUMNUS, feeling a particular debt of gratitude, pauses to pay tribute to him, tq offer prayers for the re­pose of his soul and to ask innimierable readers to do likewise. RIP—Eds.] 
BEV. JOHN C. McGINN. CS. C 

REV. ROBERT J. WAICHtn-IS, C.S.C., '41, a missionary in Bengal, was drowned on May 26, 1948. He was buried at Ranikhong, East Pakistan; India. 
Bom Aug. 3, 1918, Father Waichulis was orduocd to the pncsthcmd on June 10, 1945. He is survived by a brother and a sister. 
REV. AUGUST J. RATH, ' •y pastor of St. Boniface Church, Elmont, L. I., N. Y., died on Aug. 28. He was 68 years old. 
Ordained to the priesthood in 1912 at St. John Seminary, Brooklyn, N. Y., Father Rath served in parishes in Brooklyn until 1929, when he was as^gn­ed to St. Patrick's Church, Southold, L. I. He was assigned to his final pastorate in 1931. 
Father Rath was buried in the St. Boniface par­ish cemetery on Sept. 1, following the requiem &bss. He is survived by two brothers. 
KLEOPHAS N. BARDZH., '23, of Pittsburgh, Pa., died suddenly in Brooklyn, N. Y., on Sept. U. A member of the legal division of the Veterans Administration in Pittsburgh, he was ^^iting in Brooklyn when he flied. 
"K" was a student in both the Harvard Law School and the Duquesne Law School after he left Notre IJame. During the late war he served mth the United States Coast Guard, much of the time aboard a destro^'cr escort. 
PAUL G. FUNK, '24, lifelong resident of Elgin. III., and an attorney there for 23 years, died in the Presbyterian Hospital, Chicago, on Sept. 6, follow­ing a brief illness. He is survived by his wfife, a son and a daughter, his father and two sisten. 
An outstanding journalist while he was still a sto­dent at Notre Dame and an editor of the Notre Dame Dailyt Paul was later sports editor, for a short time, of the £f£in Courier. IVactidng law, he became one of the leading lawyers of his area, a> a member of the firm of Beverly, Funk and Odifaen. A member of the K. of C , the Elks and the Eagles, he was also a director of the Catholic Charities, -n troop committeeman for the Boy Scouts and active in the Izaak Walton League, the Elgin Assodation of Commerce and the St. Edward's Father's Chib. 
Paul was buried in Mt. Hope cemetery* E^n, following the requiem Mass ini St. Mary's Chordi.

The retirement of FATHER ERNEST DAVIS, C.S.C., was announced recently. Father Da\-is, who had ser\ed the University of Portland continuously for 24 >xars, first came to the then Columbia Uni­versity in 1909 and remained until 1912. Returning to Notre Dame, he taught there for the following 12 >*ears, until 1924. Father Davis was honored at a small diimer in the University Club in Portland where his appointment as professor emeritus of chem­istry was announced. He is now living in Corby Hall at Notre Dame.

SPOTLIGHT ALUMNUS 
EDWARD W. MEHBEN, '32 

EDWARD W. MEHREX, '32, president of the Squirt Company, Beverely Hills, Calif., was recently elected first president of a new organization, the National Brands Soft Drink Institute, representing 2,500 independent bottlers of soft drinks in the United States. 
The countrj-'s foremost advocate of some "in-between coins," like 2'/=, 7'/= and 12'/j­cent pieces, Ed has written and talked ex­tensively in his campaign, and traveled thousands of miles since early in 1948. On July 27, two identical bills providing for the coinage of 7-cent pieces were introduced in the House of Representatives, and the same bills will be reintroduced in the 8Ist Congress. 
"There is e\'idence on every hand," Ed writes, "that pricing of low-priced commo­dities is based more on convenience than on value. . . . We demand convenience and convenience coins to such an extent that we are content to pay more than we should for what we buy. . . . The addition of several more convenience-coins to the currency would tend to bring pricing in periods of relative stability closer to actual values. The resulting savings to consumers would be con­siderable. . . . When a nickel product can no longer sell for a reasonable profit at five cents, the price all too frequently jumps to the next convenience-coin .. . a dime." 
Thus Ed's campaign. You'll hear and see more of it.

From KID ASHE: 
FATHER GEORGE FISCHER, C.S.C., has been appointed a trustee of the latest college to come under operation of the Holy Cross Congregation. The institution carries the name of Stonehtll Col­lege, and already is being called "Notre Dame of the East." It is located at North Easton, Mass., on the former Fred Lathrop Ames estate. 
JIM McCABE of Los Angeles is now affiliated wiih the Beau Brummel Neckwear Co. Upon very special occasions, he has been seen wearing a red beret. When STEPHEN EUGENE CARAiODY reads this, he will make the welkin ring with some high-sounding descriptive adjectives. 
BUCK SHAW's San Francisco '49'ers of the All America Football Conference are undefeated thus far in the season's play. As this is written, they are tied with the Cleveland Browns for the top position in league standing, and are second to none in home attendance.
