From JOHN HURLEY: Believe it or not, boj-s—In 21 months we'll have been graduated 25 years. . . . Pick up your '25 Dome and give It a once-over—I just finished doing it! Say a prayer for those fellows, out of a class of 360, who have gone on ahead of us—^DUKE CLAN­CY, BOB FLYNN, RABEY FUNK, MAX HAUSER, GEORGE KING, OCK LAVERY, AL XL\THEY, BILL NEVILLE, MAURICE SMITH, DR. JOHN WEIBEL, DR. FABIAN BURKE, JIM DIVER. DR. WILBUR EATON, VINCE HARRINGTON, NOBLE KIZER, WILLARD JONES and DOC CONNELL. (If I missed anybody, I'm sorry. Please tell me about it.) 
Our class %%'as alwa>-s the biggest and best, whether it ^vas football (those famous Four Horsemen and Seven Mules, in case the youngsters who came after the depression years don't remember). Or debating— shades of RAY CUNNINGHAM, JOE HOGAN and JOHN DROEGE. Or orators like HARRY Mc-GUIRE. 
And up to that time, who ever got out a Dome like JACK SCALLAN's? And there was the Notre Dame Daily—a thing they don't have today, not to say anything about The Juggler (jokebook to you youngsters) and the Santa Maria (even our Council 1477 had a quarterly magazine). The ALUMNUS started In our time (JIM ARMSTRONG is a '25er, you know, and the best alumni secretary in the nation)! And The Catalyzer for the chemists was the only magazine of Its kind In collegiate circles. 
Remember McGLTIKE and BUTCH HAECKER with "one Issue of Pam*? " Even BISHOP 0*HARA got his start in our day with his "Religious Bulle­tin." It was the only independent publication on the campus and It had no advertisers. To quote the '25 Dome, "its harp has but two strings, its score has but two notes, its song has but two words—• vice and virtue." 
^Von't it be fun to throw our chests out amongst ourselves—because tlie other classes won't believe us anyhow! 
Let's make plans now! 

This ii nows—there is going to be a change—the University can't handle the large Commencement classes and the tremendous turn-«.ut fci the reunions at the same time. So another time umst be chosen between graduation and the opening of school. 
It will probably have to be the weekend after graduation, or as some fellows think, the weekend of Labor Day. The latter date seems good because there is alwa>-s "the free day'* of Labor Day, with Its long weekend for fellows from a great distance. You can arrange \-acations at this time better than June—the faculty will be coming back, fresh from a rest and the possibility of seminars for business and professional men Is a possibility worth con­sidering. Golf tournaments and a preview of the new football teams would be another incenti\-e. 
Let's hear from you and I mean you^ each and every one of you. 
Your secretary hasn't received a letter from a soul since he got out of the Red Cross in 1945, but I've seen PAUL DOOLEY, BOB PIERCE, RAY CUNNINGHAM, BEN KESTING, DON AHLLER, JIM ARMSTRONG and RAY TILLMAN. AU are well and all except Jim are in the Toledo area. 
Since the Army game is no more, I miss seeing JOHN BARTLEY, BILL BELL and BUGS WAL­THER. Bugs has a hot orchestra In the "Big Town" and Bartley Is in the bond business. Bill Bell was a widower, but Is married again. 
I hear that WALTER HAECKER is in Jaclson­T,-ille, Fla., with Goodrich. FRANTC ROWLAND, formerly of Alcmphls, just got a big jump in insur­ance and lives in Detroit now. CHARLIE MOUGH has a Che\Tolet agency in Sandusky, O. He was touring the west with his family this summer when I called him. 
HERMAN CENTLIVRE of Fort Wayne aad I were pnu:ticallr the only '25eis at last Jane's re­union, except South Benders like BILL CERNEY and GEORGE KOCH. George, by the war, has given some fine radio talks aloi^ Catholic Actioa lines. He is a teacher in Riley High Sdwol in Soath Bend and very active in the K. of G. He is mm of the principal figures in the South Bend K. of 
C. weekly radio program which has attracted na­tional attention. One of Bill's daughters was married not lot^; ^o. 
Herm looks great and is doii^ a fine job id Fort Wayne in advertising for a brewery, and in raisti^ some outstanding Catholic youi^ters in that Indiana town. 
Let's hear from the rest of you "boys" and teU me what you think of the new alumni weekend for June, 1950, both as to arrangements and the time. We '25ers were always changing things so we mi^ t as well keep it up—a quarter of a century later! 
Here's a \-ote for a Labor Day weekend frcnn your secretary. Speaking of votes, let's elect a new secre­tary, too! I've been in longer than the Democrats. 
P.S. Forgot to mention that we '25ers gave the first Irish terrier to the "Fightin' Irish," a gift to ROCKNE from that most active clid> on the campus—^you guessed it—the Toledo Quh. From LOUIE BUCKLEY: 

Your class secretary* was transferred Sept. 1 to the Chicago Regional Office of the Federal Security Agency, 188 W. Randolph St., Chicago 1, as Associ­ate Regional Representative. Since this region in­cludes Illinois, Wisconsin, Indiana and Minnesota, I hope to see '28 men occasionally on trips to St. Paul and Indianapolis. I trust the '28 men in the Chicago area, as well as those of you who visit Chicago, will keep me currently adrised of news for this column. 
The "Garber Poll' article in the September-Octo­ber 1948 ALUMNUS was very* well done. We appre­ciate the fine work you did on this project, Bernie, and assume you will make another poll, and receive an even greater response, from the '28 men in 1953 for our 25-year reunion. 
The '28 man at the reunion who prepared the notice proposing tliat the class of '28 sponsor the idea of a Gaudete Afedal would like to have the reaction of additional classmates to the plan. Since he prefers to remain anon>Tnous, I will be pleased to fon\'ard your comments to him on this proposal. The suggestion, in brief, is that the University award to one alumnus each year a Gaudete Medal in recognition of his courage in meeting evcry-day personal problems. 
BILL KEARNEY, chairman of the '28 Golf Tour­nament, called my attention to some additional un­finished business from our reunion weekend. Bill writes as foUou-s: "On Sunday, in the rain, I went over to the golf shanty to see who' was the fiham­pion golfer in our class, but either a tournament sheet from our class had not been posted as directed or it had been taken down by someone. As a result, I have an engraved silver plate to be presented to the champion of our class, but do not know who it is. If you have any idea' who might be entitled to it, if you will drop me a card, I will immediately send this trophy." Since I am unable to give BiU an answer on this one, how about hearing from the '28 golfers as to what should be done with the trophy?

JIM CANIZARO sent along the following notes, based on his reunion contacts, from Jackson, Miss., where he is an architect :"My trip to Notre Dame was very eventful in that I hadn't been back for a reunion for nearly 18 years. I met a lot of faces that I didn't recognize at first, but after introducing mj-self I found that these bo\-s were very close friends of mine at Notre Dame. Some had changed and others had not. 
"I was very happy to see CHET RICE from Cleveland, who was civil engineer-bred but is now selling building materials and is still as big a shot as ever. 
"BOB KIRBY looked as if he was ready to take the field and lead the bo^•s in a cheer; except for a few gray hairs he u-as the same as I remember him back in 1928.

FRANK CONNORS reported from Arcade, N. Y., where he is city clerk-treasurer. I understand he was recently elected president of the Municipal Electric Utilities /Vssociation of New York State. Frank writes as follo\vs: 
"There is little that I can add to what has al­ready been said about the best reunion e\-er except to add just another pat on the back to each and cver>' committee member and especially WILLARD WAGNER for a very fine job. 
"It was swell to see so many again and I shall look forward to the 2oth hoping to sec many more that were unable to make the 20th. 
"I was particularly fortunate in being able to drive from BufTalo with GUS JENKINS, BERNIE BIRD and MARTY RYAN, thereby making a full week of the reunion. 
"Unfortunately I haven't seen nor heard from any of the felIo\%-s since the reunion except for one pleasant surprise. The Municipal Electric Utilities Association of New York State held their annual convention at Alexandria Bay, N. Y., during the early part of September. One of the principal speak­ers turned out to be FRANCIS L. DUQUETTE who is district meter specialist for the General Electric Company. This was the first time that I have seen Frank since 1928 and so we had a lot to talk about in a rather limited time."

PATRICK J. FISHEa '33 
PATRICK J. FISHER, '36, past president 

and present executive secretary of the Notre Dame Club of Indianapolis, is also grand knight of Indianapolis Council, No. 437, Knights of Columbus. 
And just to be sure that he is busy he is also a member of the Catholic Activities Committee, Te Deum International, and the Catholic Evidence Guild. 
A Knight of Columbus for 14 years, Pat ser\'cd as advocate, chancellor, and deputy grand knight of Indianapolis Council before he was chosen last June for the top post in the organization. During World War II he was in the Army, entering as a private and coming out as a captain. 
Pat is a practicing attorney, with oflSces in the Indiana Trust BIdg., Indianapolis. He and his wife are the parents of three chil­dren.

In 1&46. the year HUGH DeVORE left Notre Dame to coach football at St. Bonavcnture College in western New York, that Franciscan college had an enroUinent of less than 1.400. Today, close to 2,200 undergraduates alone have jammed the school until it is literally bursting at the scams. 
Hughie isnH responsible for all that increase of course, but the Devore following is huge and faith­ful. An idol of the Bonaventure students, Hugh was presented with a shiny new DeSoto by them in appreciation of his work last >'ear. Out of recogni­tion to him. a local benefactor has contributed a $100,000 stadium to the college. He has turned down repeated coaching ofTers from major collegiate and professional outfits. 
In Hugh's first >'ear at Bonaventure, the Bonnies won a bid to the Tobacco Bowl. losing a thriller to Muhlenberg, 26-25. Last year, the Indians won six and lost three fantastically close ones to bring Hugh's two-year record to a total of 12 victories against four losses in regular season play. This j-car, he scheduled the toughest slate in the school's histor>', Bonavcn-

A few da>-s ago a letter from long time faithful correspondent DANIEL P. FALSIONI, attorney, of Lockport, N. Y. Dino was just elected grand knight of his council and of course has been kept busy by that one job. Keep up the good work and don't let the letters stop. 
Now the important news. Just in case youVe feel­ing the rax'ages of age, be reminded that it's only natural. You have of course foi^otten it, but ten >-ears ago next June you went forth into a world that probably couldn't have been worse, clutching that old sheepskin and looking for a job, even as most of us. Then the late unpleasantness with Adolph and the Little Men, and your ten years are up. All of which is to say that come June you are supposed to show your face at what will certainly be the granddaddy of all reunions. This will be a two-in-one affair, the argument over the water hav­ing forced a postponement of the five-year get-together. So make your plans now, let nothing deter you and we'll be seeing you.

WALTER H. lOHNSON. IB.. '39 

WALTER H . JOHNSON, JR., '39 formerly director of sales for American Airlines' east-em region, has assumed new duties as re­gional vice president. The region embraces Canada and ten East Coast states. 
Walt served with the airline industry's first cargo sales and traffic staff and helped pioneer American's cargo sales department. He established Amercican's first' cargo field ofiSce, in Chicago, was organizer of the air­line's cargo operations in Mexico, and par­ticipated in the planning of American's air­freight service. 
During the war, Walt served for two years in the Marine Corps, his final assignment having been in the Pacific on the staff of the Third Marine Air Wing commander. He joined American Airlines' sales department in New York City in 1940. His headquarters are in New York.

SPOTLIGHT ALUMNUS 
LT. JOSEPH H. EWING. '32 

1ST LT. JO-SEPH H . EWING, '32, Wash­ington, D. C, son of Neal H. Ewing, '84, Roselle, N. J., is the author of "29, Let's Go," an illustrated history of the 29th In­fantry Division in which the former served in Europe in World War II. 
Grandnephew of General William Tecimi­seh Sherman, Lt. Ewing comes by his mili­tary interest naturally. He is following a military career with the historical division of the Department of the Army in Wash­ington. 
Lt. Ewing wrote the history while he was on active duty in the army, but it was pub­lished by a private publisher, the Infantry Journal. It is the heroic story of a combat group that took heavy losses without break­ing and contains more than 30 maps and 200 photographs. 
GABRESKI TOP ACE 
tt. Col. Francis S. Gabreski. '42. 
. top United States flying ace in World War n, was one of three flyers hon­ored on Sept. 25 at the first Air Force reunion in Madison Square Garden, New York City. The re­union was a highlight oi the con­vention of the Air Force JUsodation. Col. tiabresld, a member of the Eighth Air Force, was acclaimed for shooting down 28 planes. Major Robert S. lohnson and Col. Chorles 
H. MacOonald. who shot down 27 planes apiece, were also honored. All three men exceeded the record of Capt. Eddie Rickenbacker, lead­ing ace of World War I, who shot down 26 planes.

We ha\ie lifted one paragraph from PhiFs good letter which we would like to give you here, for it is worth considering separately: 
"One thing irks mc whenever I receive a commu­nique from Notre Dame. That is to sec mj-self listed as 'J. P. Cbrke, '45.* Maybe that is necessary for record purposes. But I want to attend my re­unions with the lads I lived with: those who entered 
N.D. in September, 1940. So let's keep our class together, r^ardlcss of ^vhether a man got his de­gree in *43 or *47, or not at all. Next year is OUR five->-ear reunion!" 
Thanks, Phil. Now comes the caU for volunteers. First of all, if you want that 44 tag on your Alumni Association record, let your secretary* know, or write directly to the .-Mumni Office at Xotre Dame. Things have been straighiening themselves out grad­ually, but we know that many of the class are still carried along on the rosters of '43 through '48. It has just been a matter of slotting you in the calen­dar >'ear that you graduated, regardless of when you started at Notre Dame. Indiridual contact is the only way the record can be corrected, and the 
•way things stand it has to be up to each man to notify the front office one way or another. If you want to be '44, and you aren't now, it's up to you. (AVc second the motion. TTie war years have inevitably made for confusion, but we are glad to put a man with the class of his choice, regardless of the year of his degree, if we arc notified ac­cordingly.—Ed.] 
Volunteers now for that reunion in '49. Your secretary, having drifted as far west as California, is not going to be able to get back for the glad handing. And since reunions need chairmen, com­mittees and some sort of organization, we are hop­ing that the local lads (South Bend-Chicago area) will come forth and offer to whip things up. Any volunteers? 
And for everyone it is time enough that you began planning your way in and out of South Bend our reunion weekend. Dates haven't been set yet, but June has been the usual month. Watch this column and watch the ALUMNUS for the details, as well as we can give them.

SPOTLIGHT ALUMNUS 

A. I. PORTA, '25 

A. J. PORTA, '25, comptroller of The Studebaker Corporation, South Bend, has been associated with this company since he was graduated from Notre Dame. He began his career as a billing clerk and progressed through virtually every division of the ac­counting department before being appointed comptroller of The Studebaker Pacific Cor­poration, Ix)s Angeles, Calif., in 1939. Two years later he was named chief accountant of the parent organization in South Bend; in 1944 he was elected assistant comptroller and became comptroller in 1947. 
Mrs. Porta, a former Studebaker em­ploye, was before her marriage in Novem­ber, 1928, Miss Mildred Martindale, of South Bend. They have two children: John, 16; and Sharon, 10. John has applied for admission to Notre Dame in September, 1949. Sharon attends the junior school at St. Mar>''s College. 
A native of Fort Smith, Ark., the Stude­baker comptroller passed the Indiana CPA examination in November, 1935. He is a member of the American Institute of Ac­countants, the Controllers Institute of Amer­ica and the South Bend Rotary Club.

From HER-\fA.V ZITT: 

Tragedy has claimed the life of the second 1948 grad. ED LcCOUTEUR, A.B., in August, died at Hines Veterans* Hospital. Chicago. Sept. 19 after an illness of six weeks. Ed had been a resident of Vetvillc with his wife and son. Death resulted from complications that had set in as a result of an air­plane crash he suffered in cadet training during the war. Ed's heart v»-as ucakened by an injurj' to his leg, and the injury acted as a strain in his heart every step he took. Let's all offer up our prayers for Ed, his wife and son. 
TO^f BUO.VO's father has offered a $5,000 re­ward for information leading to the arrest and con­viction of the person or persons responsible for his son's death. On Nfay 20, after several earlier ses­sions, a coroner's jur>" in Chicago finally termed the death caused by drowning, complicated by bar­biturate poisoning.

And Onlr 10 Yean Ago: 
The University opened its 97th year with a student enrollment of 3100. • * • Con­struction on the Rockne Memorial Field House neared completion. • • • Some of the world's foremost scholars gathered at Notre Dame to attend a Symposium on Political and Social Philosophy. Among addresses given were those by Jacques Maritain, Des­mond Fitzgerald, Yves R. Simon, and Mor­timer J. Adler. • • » The 2Ist annual Laymen's Retreat, held on the campus, Aug. 4 to 7. attracted 1,200 men. « • • "Room Service," grand old drama complete \»ith ice-bags and moose-heads, was presented by the University Theatre. * * * The first issue of The Review of Politics, Notre Dame quarterly dealing with political theories and realities, appeared on local newsstands. • * • Miss Ethel Barrymore, one of the most famous names of the American theatre, led the Jitney Players through a rollicking performance of "Dear Old Alma Mater," classic comedy of American college life. » • • Father John F. O'Hara, C.S.C, pres­ident of the University, sailed for South .\merica as one of a delegation headed by Secretary of State Cordell Hull to represent this country in the Pan American confer­ence at Lima, Peru. 

Natural Law Institute 
The second annual Natural Law Insti­tute will be held at Notre Dame, Friday and Saturday, Dec. 10 and U. 
The program will be devoted to the his­toric development of the natural law and to an exposition of the current conditions of the natual law philosophy in American jur­isprudence. Last year's Natural Law Insti­tute was sponsored by the Notre Dame Club of New York. This year's institute is being sponsored by Alvin A. Gould, O., business­man. The sessions will be open to the public. 
Do your friends subscribe to THE REVIEW OF POLITICS? Now completing its tenth year. 
S 

A Notre Dame Contribution to American Culture 
Canon Bernard Iddings Bell says: 
"The Review of Politics is, in these days of confused political thinking and consequent action leading toward disaster and woe, an exceedingly useful journal. Definitely CathoUc, it is never obscurantist or sentimen­tal. It is informed about facts and yet does not lose sight of that which is deeper than facts. I find that I must read it and that I enjoy reading it." 
Editor: Waldcmar Gurian Managing Editors: Thomas T. McAvoy, C.S.C. and Frank O'Malley 

The Review of Politics publishes articles on national and international affairs and on religion, history, philosophy and other fields of Christian culture by outstanding Amer­ican and European scholars.

Needs of Notre Dome 
These are requirements summarized by Rev. Robert H. Sweeney, c.s.c, executive assistant to the President, for ade­quate care of the present enroUnjent of 4,500 students. They do not represent the ultimate permanent development of the University. It is obvious that the amounts suggested here are approximations, subject to variations in building costs, wages, and investment returns. 
As indicated in the February issue of FORTUNE magazine, an article entitled "Alma Mater Asks for Two Billion Dollars" lists Notre Dame as needing 25 million for building e-xpansion, research, and scholarships. 
I. BUILDINGS 

1. Residence Halls to house properly the present student-body: 
a)
 Graduate Students' Hall. 


b)
 Four residence halls for undergraduates. 


2.
 Student Union Building: rooms for student clubs and activities; ballroom for dances and gatherings; refreshment center for students, their families and friends; dining facilities. 

3.
 College of Arts and Letters Building: classrooms, conference and seminar rooms for philosophy, history, languages, social sciences and other A.B. courses. 

4.
 Fine Arts Building: to include auditorium for en­tire student body and graduations; facilities for Departments of Music, Art, Radio and Communi­cations, Speech, and Dramatics. 

5.
 Chemistry Building: classrooms and laboratories for teaching and research, including atomic energy laboratories. 

6.
 Memorial Librarj': present library to be reserved for Mediaeval Institute, archives and art gallery. 

7.
 Bacteriology Laboratories: for research in germ-free life, cancer, control of disease germs. 

8.
 Physics Building: classrooms; research laborato­ries in electronics, nuclear physics, etc. 

9.
 Field House: to replace present gymnasium. 

10.
 Memorial Chapel: in honor of the war dead. 


II. SUPPORT AND AUGMENTING OF TEACHING STAFF: additional training of present teachers and addition of outstanding scholars to the staff is needed to keep the faculty a first-rate teaching group. Notre Dame must raise endo\vment for: (a) salaries of teachers engaged in research; (b) professorships in each Department offering higher degrees; (c) dis­tinguished service professorships; (d) funds to bring in visiting professors and special lecturers; (e) funds to support teachers making special studies at other cen­ters of learning. All of these are an integral part of a University that is improving the quality of its teaching. 
IIL STUDENT AID PROGRAM: to support needy stu­dents of superior calibre. This involves: (a) scholar­ships for the Graduate School; (b) Post-doctoral scholarships, for exceptional scholars to work at Notre Dame after taking doctorates; (c) Scholarships for needy imdergraduates whose, exceptional talents make them highly desirable as members of the alumni. Note: A permanent 4-year scholarship for a deserving student requires a fund of some $50,000; a pre-doc­toral fellowship, $65,000; a post-doctoral fellowship, $150,000. 
IV. SPECIAL FUNDS: are highly desirable to provide 
(a)
 for Teachers Retirement and Sick Relief; (b) for Employees' Pensions and Sick Relief; (c) for pur­chase of new books and upkeep of the Library; (d) for support of the learned publications, such as The Review of Politics, The American Midland Natural­ist and the others. 

V.
 GENERAL UNRESTRICTED ENDOWMENT: to meet emergencies and unforeseen opportunities. There are now some 25 imiversities in the country that have more than $20,000,000 of endowment. Notre Dame has $4,214,294.49, and ranks ninety-fifth. We should like to see her among the first ttventy-five. 


Foreword 

UH€^ iJwl€Mt€€€l€ comprises a basic "set of tools" with which city committees and alumni may fashion their programs of personal solici­tation for the Foundation in 1948. 
Everything is here . . . lists of alumni donors and non-alumm donors .. . by states and by cities .. . lists of non-contributing alumni ... gifts by states and cities ... in number and amount... by alumni and non-alumni . . . and other helpful analyses of the financial record of 1947, the Foundation's inaugural year .. . all to be made use of as you see fit, to meet your.o^vn local situation in 1948. 
This is the soil upon which we stand . . . with its good record of productivity last year . . . wt h its rich potentailities for a far more abundant harvest this year. 
It is soil which needs a lot of tilling—intensively in the first months of the year—more extensively in the latter months. 
First must come the now-traditional "cultivation of our own gar­den"—confirmation and solicitation of the lists of our own alumni and non-graduate former students, ^vith a steady, continuous effort to encourage a full 100% participation and the raising of our financial sights. 
And then there Avill follow a fuller concentration upon the prepar­ation of lists, in each community, of those non-alumni friends of Notre Dame who are potential contributors—lists which will be mailed to the Campus Staff and "serviced" from there in cooperation with the local committees. 
It is to give you encouragement, and a base, that we have indi­cated our present small, but loyal and generous, group of friends in bold face type in the list of 1947 contributors. They are shining ex­amples for others. 
First we pte... and then we get. But still in your own way—and at your own convenience. 
The Job of the City Committee 
We cannot repeat too often that the effectiveness of the Foundation's plan depends on the abiUty and activity of each City Committee. The chairman of a City Committee and his committee members consti­tute the "guiding spirit" of the entire operation. The sum total of all City Committee efforts will deter­mine the future position of Notre Dame University. It is a challenge .. . it is a great responsibility. 
The foIlo^^ing outline was prepared to give suggestions as to methods in the attainment of maximum results. It is not intended to be dictum, for ultimate results will depend largely on the spontaneity of each committee member. Nevertheless, the suggestions made are worthy of careful and sympathetic study. Whatever plan you adopt, ultimately, should receive your best efforts and enthusiasm. 

%Hp f . . . ANALYZE YOUR JOB 
The size of the job varies form city to city. Each city will require an organization to meet local re­quirements. What is the number of Alumni? How many former students, non-graduates? How many friends from your city are listed with the University (the names are indicated in the Chair­man's "Kit")? How many Alumni have been supporting the University? How many should be contacted personally, etc.? This type of a review is essential for the next step. 

Step II . , , PLAN YOUR ORGANIZATION 
The organization, in size and character, will depend on the size of the job and the objectives to be accomplished. Your analysis of the job should indicate the personnel needed. If the initial commit­tee needs to be enlarged by a sub-committee, plans should be laid for additional enlistments, then determine the duties and assign them to each member. 

Step Iff . . . PLAN AND PREPARE YOUR PROCEDURE 
Concurrently, while you are building your organization, plan and outline the procedure you expect to follow. When meetings of the Conmiittee are held, the chairman should explain in detail just what is expected of the Committee collectively and individually. Consider the "special contacts" to be made with Alumni, with friends, and who should make them. 
Step fV . . . THE JOB 
A. Review the list of Alumni — note the amount subscribed by each. Should any of them give more? If so, who should make the approach? Note especially those who have not supported the University during the past and the current year. Plan an approach and assign each name to a member of the local committee's organization for piersonal interview. 
B. Review the Ust of former students. Go over this list carefully to determine their current ad­dress. All addresses known to be incorrect should be reported to the Campus Office of the Foundation. Plan to contact all who live in your city. Invite them to join with other Notre Dame-men in support of the University. Follow the same procedure in planning contacts as you planned for the Alumni — the difference will be in approach, as this group is not so fa­miUar with current developments as the Alumni. 
Important Note: The cooperation of Local clubs should be enlisted by City Committees of which the Local Club President is an ex-officio member, as the Club may consider making con­tacts with all graduates and non-graduate Notre Dame men in the area. Also, note under Step IV 'T)" how each Club may assist in securing the names of "friends" from among the CathoUc population in the area. 
C. Review the list of ''friends of the University." Note those who have made gifts this year, and those who have not. Determine who should call personally on those who have not given during the current year. This list should be reviewed carefully — assignments for persoi^ contacts made to the best person possible. 
D. The three categories above give you the names of those who are on record at the University. Now turn attention to those "Friends andPotential Friends" who are not recorded at the Uni­versity. 
To bring them on record, the following suggested procedure is submitted: Ask the City Club President to assign one or two members from each of the parishes in your area the job of re­viewing the membership of each parish to select those who they feel are, or should be, interested in Notre Dame. (This should not be considered as an official parish survey.) 
Since the University will spend time and funds in cultivating the interest of those whose names are submitted to the University, the list should be screened by the local committee to eliminate those whose interest may prove nebulous. 
This list of "friends" should be submitted as prospects whom the Committee will ultimately call upon personally. The names are needed as quickly as possible for cultivation by literatiure, let­ters, etc. After screening, the list should be forwarded to the Campus Office of the Foundation. (Forms are provided for this purpose. It would prove helpful if certain pertinent information is supplied with each name, as indicated on the form.) 
There are many friends among non-Catholics who are interested in Notre Dame. Special pro­vision should be made to secure these names and with the same disposition as indicated above with Catholics. 
Some Corporations are potential supporters of the University. The University Foundation de­sires their names also. 
Many private foundations have been created during the past few years. These foundations have been created for charitable and educational purposes. Some may be in your community. If so, the names of all such foundations should be supplied to the Notre Dame Foundation. 
Step V . . . REPORTING 

All remittances should be sent by the City Committee or donor direcdy to "The University of Notre Dame Foundation, Main Building, Notre Dame, Indiana." All checks should be made payable to "The University of Notre Dame." Gifts of securities should also be made to the University. 
A receipt will be sent to each donor by the President of the University. 
Step Vf . . . SUMMARY IN REVIEW 
1.
 Make your Committee representative. 

2.
 Choose and enlist your personnel. 

3.
 Brief your personnel. 


4.
 Make full use of all educational material. 

5.
 See individually each person to be called upon. (There is no substitute for personal contacts.) 

6.
 Set a high standard. 

7.
 Report progress. 



1^ 
8. Follow through until the job is done. 
7 

City Committee Program for 1948 
First Six Months — 1. Secure 100 per cent participation of alumni, including non-graduates, in the Sixth Annual Alumni Fund phase of the Foundation.  
2. Emphasize contact wdth those alumni who are not listed as participating in 1947, through early contact for correctness of address, or any other reason which might logically interfere with con­tributions.  
3. Study methods of increasing contributions, in amounts. We are not trying to exert pressure to secure contributions beyond individual means, but full information of the University's needs should result in many increases, as should the use of the gift cards providing for deferred giving through the year.  i  
4. Confact wherever possible the non-graduate former student.come in the Alumni Association if interested.   These men are ehgible to and wel­ 
5. Secure from the personal contacts with the Notre Dame men above the names of non-alumni Friends of the Universitj' of Notre Dame, who may be interested in the Foundation program.  
Second Six Months —  
1. Follow up and uncontacted alumni or non-graduate former students toward the 100 per cent Notre Dame participation.  
2. Coflfocf and bmld the list of Friends of the University of Notre Dame, keeping in mind the value of the small annual gift, the larger gifts for some special project, and the special prospect whom you may wish to have contacted directiy from the University for potential major benefactions.  %  
3. Develop the work of the Bequest and Living Trust Committee in the placing of Notre Dame in wills, trusts, etc.  
4. Survey and contact, or suggest for direct contact, local corjmrations, or private foundations, which might be sources of support for the University or any of the phases of Notre Dame's pro­gram.  

Note: In both the above periods, the Committees have a helpful and tangible suggestion for those interested in the plan to "Give or Get $200" annually suggested in the February ALUMNUS. It is not advanced as a quota or a must, but as a concrete example of how a magniiicent job can be done collectively through relatively modest individual action. J

"In terms of the forty-billion-dollar Federal budget they are discussing here, the $900,000 towards our three-million-dollar goal looks insignificant. But I believe that few of those billions are going to have as profound an effect for good on this na­tion as these millions that are being given voluntarily to independent educational institutions such as Washington and Lee, which happens to be my alma mater. 
"These independent universities are fighting today to maintain their freedom from governmental subsidy, because they know their academic liberty depends on it. Their struggle should prompt immediate assistance from American business men, both as corporations and as individuals wt h a stake in'free enterprise. 
"Business and industry need now, and are going to need more in the futitfe, collie graduates trained in a tradition of independence of Government subsidy and Government dictation." 
JOHN W. DAVIS, Washington & Lee, '92

Outlook for 1948 Contributions 
The Alumni Fund has grown consistently from year to year. *For the Centenary Fund of 1941-43, 3462 alumni contributed $107,424.13. In 1947 the number of alumni contributing doubled to 7887, and the amount more than trebled to $346,013.72. From the list of alumni being checked for correct addresses, and interested former students, the Alumni Fimd for 1948 should again take a big jump in the number of alumni participating, as well as in the amount contributed.' 
The potential in the field of "Friends" of the University is un­limited. In 1941, 24 friends contributed $22,868.00. In 1947, the number of friends and amount increased NINE times to 203 and $205,490.00 respectively. This makes an average of one friend per chairman city, which could easily be increased from FIVE to TWEN­TY-FIVE times. Already in 1948 we have ELEVEN new friends in Cheyenne, Wyoming, and SIX in Green Bay, Wisconsin. 
In addition to gifts to the Notre Dame Foimdation, the University of Notre Dame also acknowledges gifts for fellowships, industrial and government research, the Outdoor Advertising Foundation, and ma­chinery with a known valuation for 1947 of $559,000.00. Also, gifts have been received in the form of art and books to the Mediaeval Institute and the Library with a valuation of many thousands of dollars. ..tlt 
City and State totals will be found in the Master Table, pages 9 to 17. 
The following hst of givers in 1947 is submitted to express again the appreciation of the University of Notre Dame to its many loyal alumni and friends.

Alumni to be Checked for the 1948 Directory 
(Note: The following names which did not appear in the 1947 list of Foundation con­tributors, should be checked carefully by the City Committees for correct addresses. The -•Uumni Director)' to be published in 1948 should find a maximum of correct addresses. Many instances in the years immediately following the war have indicated that alumni have moved, or are still in school or in service, or that the alumnus himself is not at the address but mem­bers of the family receive his mail there with complications of. forwarding. To give our Director)- its maximum efiBcienc)', and to insure the best contacts with alumni, the foUowng persons should be contacted for their proper address, to be forwarded to the Foundation Office.)

PERTINENT FACTS about Notre Dame 
Organization 

The University of Notre Dame was founded in 1842. It is conducted by the Congregation of Holy Cross. (Congregatio a Sancta Cruce). The University includes Colleges of (a) Arts and Jitters, (b) Science, (c) Engineering, (d) Law, 
(e) Commerce, and the Graduate School. There are 33 departments in the undergraduate school and the Graduate School offers the Ph.D. degree in eleven departments — Philosophy, Eng­lish, History, Political Science, Biology, Chemistr}-, Physics, Mathematics and Metallurgy. The present president. Reverend John J. Cavanaugh, C.S.C., is the fourteenth president, taking office in July, 1946. Present enrollment (1948) totals 4,500 students. The faculty numbers 433, of whom three-fourths are laymen. Fifty buildings are used on the 1700 acre campus for University purposes. The Notre Dame dining halls, constructed in 1927, serve 15,000 meals daily during the school year. There are 14 libraries on the campus, serving general. College and departmental needs, with 250,000 volumes, many of them rare special collections, such as the Dante and Zahm and the Nieuwland and Greene libraries. Fourteen growing laboratories, including the famous Laboratories of Bacteriology (LOBUND), serve the Colleges of Science and Engineering. The University is a member of the North Central Association of Colleges and Universities, of the National Catholic Educational Association, of the 
Association of American Colleges, and is accred­
ited by the Association of American Universities. 
There are thirteen residence halls on the campus. It has always been the policy* of the University to house students on the cahipus. Present facilities require supplementing for permanent enrollment at the present figure by some four residence halls, and dining hall facilities proportionately. Three present residence halls need replacement 
Enrollment in 1905 was 210 students in the Uni­versity; in 1925, 2156 students; in 1940, 3343 students. 
Present research includes the highly significant germ-free technique of the Bacteriology Department, nu­clear ph)^ics, electronic anti-malarial drugs, nutri­tion projects including vitamin study and the causes of tooth decay, and others, aided by the germ-free technique and by equipment such as the powerful 4,000,000-volt electrostatic generator. 
The summer school, now eight weeks in duration, which has graduated hundreds of nuns, priests, and brothers of various orders, was begun in 1918. 
(1947 enrolhnent, 1752). 

The Bureau of Economic Research has published a number of studies which have attracted wide atten­tion in finance and industry. 
Academic Tradition 
The College of Law was the' first permanent depart­ment in that field organized in a Catholic college in America (1869). 
The department of civil engineering, also a pioneer Catholic educational departure, was organized in 1875. 
Some of the most significant experiments in aeronau­tical science were performed at Notre Dame in the '80s by Prof. Albert Zahm. 
The first successful wireless message in thu country was sent by Prof. Jerome Greene from Notre Dame to Saint Mary's in May, 1899. 
The basic fonnulae for synthetic rubber came_ from Notre Dame laboratories through the genius of Rev. Julius Nieuwland, C.S.C., supplying the basis for Dupont's development of Neoprene. 
The Mediaeval Institute, founded in 1946, is based on research and publications in previous years, and is projected as a great contribution to modem Christian civilization. 
Hisforical 

The Laetare Medal was established in 1883. The Wightman Art Gallery is one of the best art collections in the Middle West, occupying six rooms on the second floor of the University Library. In World War II, Notre Dame contributed 10,000 men to the services, and devoted the facilities of the campus to training and research. The Naval Midshipman School commissioned 12,000 trainees at Notre Daine. Notre Dame's gold stars in World War I numbered 56, in World War II, 327. Father Stephen Badin, first priest ordained in the United States and one-time owner of the site of the University, is buried in the replica of the Log Chapel on the campus where he pursued his In­dian mission work. 
The Laymen's Retreat (1947 attendance, 1500). A three-day retreat for Catholic men from the Mid­dle West, one of the largest of its kind, was insti-
Of Genera/ Interest 
The great fire which almost destroyed Notre Dame occurred in April, 1879. 
A part of both the annual student body and faculty are non-Catholic, one of the rich and favorite traditions of Notre Dame, incorporating as it does both tolerance and democracy. 
Notre Dame students, as in other colleges and uni­versities, pay only a part of the cost of their edu­cation. The balance is made up by gifts from alumni and non-alumni, income from A e limited endowment, by income from auxiliary projects, and by the unremunerated services of the religious of the Congregation of Holy Cross, who serve as teachers and administrators tvithout salary. 
The "American Midland Naturalist" was founded at Notre Dame by Rev. Julius Nieuwland, C.S.C., almost as eminent in botany as he was in chem­istry. 
The Ave Maria, Catholic home weekly, was founded at Notre Dame in 1865 by Father Edward Sorin, founder of the University, and is now published on the campus in a modem'printing plant. 
Sorin Hall, senior residence hall, was the first student residence hall built at a Catholic school in this country (1872). 
Three-fourths, or 10,500, of Notre Dame's alumni were graduated after 1930. The first football game was played at Notre Dame in April, 1887. 
Knute Rockhe, world famous coach, was graduated from Notre Dame as an honor student in diemistry in 1914. He was head coach of football from 1917 to 1931. During that period his teams won 111 games and lost only 13 games, tying 6 games. In 58 seasons Notre Dame has won 360 games and lost only 77 games, tying 29 games. 
The Bequest and Living Trust Committee 
The primary purpose of the Bequest and Living Trust Committee is to enlist the interest of all lawyers and trust offi­cers in the community to stimulate the remembrance of Notre Dame in wills of local persons. 
Then, too, the Committee can be the source of information in the community in matters of tax deduction benefits for all donors, and in the legal aspects of corporate giving. 
The Bequest and Living Trust Committee can be invalu­able in helping the City Committee to ascertain what persons in the higher bracket should be contacted. 
Lawyers are obviously the initial choice for the Bequest and Living Trust Committee, but judges, non-practicing law­yers (law grads in business for example), bankers, accountants, insurance counsellors, and others with particular local value on this Committee may be appointed to advantage. 
Present plans call for the organization of The Notre Dame Legal Association at commencement time. The Association should be very instrumental in helping the Foundation achieve its ends. 
The brochure on wills, trusts, etc., will follow the organi­zation of the Legal Association. The brochure wiU be divided into four sections—^wills, trusts, direct gifts, and endowments. Each section will cover its topic completely, even to the point of the legal forms to be used.

1948 GIVING TO DATE 
The ateaiBi rmemriior the first six months of 1948 is certainly a gratifying one. Already 72% of the 7,887 alumni contributors of last year have contributed in 1948. Of the 5,658 aliunni contributors in 1948, 25% -are new donors—alumni who 'did not contribute at all in 1947, 
StUl another 27% have increased their contribution this year over last year. 
With 7,500 alumni who have not contributed yet this year, there is more than 
just a possibility that last year's record of 7,887 alumni contributors will falL 
Tfee •••-•teMai reemri in'1948 is also one of great accomplishment. • The record of 318 friend contributors already in 1948, as compared to 203 for the entire year of 1947, speaks for itself. 
Needless to say, we have but scratched the surface of the great number of loyal friends of Notre Dame. Thousands of new friends have been suggested by the City Committees and Alimini. These friends have already received the first issue of Notre Dame, and have been placed on our mailing list for subsequent issues of Notre Dame and The Substance of Things Hoped For—a guide for benefactors of the University of Notre Dame (will be ready for distribution at the end of September). 
The list of donors, pages 15 to 40, is submitted to express again the appPidation of the University of Notre Dame to its many loyal alumni and friends. City totals will be foimd in the Master Table, pages 8 to 12. . 
Heeds of Notre Du e 
Bg the Kec. ^•fea S. C«r«M«Bsb, C.S.C. President, University of Notre Bame Director, University of Notre Dame Foundation 
The following specific needs of The University were stressed by Father Cavanaugh 
in an address delivered at the Alunuii Dinner, June 5,1948, in the Campus Dining 
HaU: 

"There are many drastic, specific needs here at Notre Dame, as you know. "One of them that can be met only by the immediate help of alumni, and friends whom you can induce to help Notre Detme, is this: (fee pr»vi»tmm •# MeMP mpmce aui taeilitiea IT hmth ear ileii«r(aieB(« ml ChmmUtrg «a 4 Ffeffaies. "Laboratory space is inadequate. Space for research is hopelessly limited. Equipment amoimting to hundreds of thousands of dollars housed in old Science Hall is in grave danger of destruction by fire. It is bur intention to construct ade­quate facilities for the work in chemistry and physics as soon as the contributions of alumni and friends make it possible. "I am utterly confident that Notre Dame can rely upon help from her alumni. I believe that in this emergency the alumni will be willing to solicit help from their non-alumni friends. 
"There is another drastic need which I should like to mention—a need which directly concerns the alumni, the parents of students, and friends of The University. 
"We have no housing facilities to take care of returning alumni and friends. I believe that this need can reasonably be called urgent. At present there is no place where the alumni may stay together on the campus or where the students may take their parents or visiting guests for comfortable dining facilities. 
''A auest hom»e . . . m X»ire Bmme INK .. . would meet such needs. We have already had preliminary plans drawn. We are very anxious to begin con­struction on such a guest house; but, again, we cannot actually start until we get help from alumni and friends." 
Tie Job 0f the City Cnijttee 
THE CITY COMMITTEE IS THE ««F01JNHATI*Iir'S F«VNHATI«Iir>' 
We cannot repeat too often that the effectiveness of the Foundation's plan depends upon the ability and activity of each City Committee. The chairman of a City Committee and his committee members constitute the "guiding spirit" of the entire operation. The sum total of all City Committee efforts will determine the future position of The University of Notre Dame. It is a challenge... it is a great responsibility. 
The following outline was prepared to give suggestions as to methods in the attainment of maximum results. It is not intended to be dictum, for ultimate results will depend largely on the spontaneity of each committee member. Never­theless, the suggestions made are worthy of Cciref ul and sympathetic study. What­ever plan you adopt, ultimately, should receive your best efforts and enthusiasm. 
STEP I Analyze Your Job 
Organization—niunber of graduates, non-graduates 
and friends of the University. 

STEP II Plan Your Organization. List of objectives—number of personnel needed. 
STEP m Plan and Prepare Your Procedure 
While building your organization, plan and outline 
the procedure you expect to follow. 

STEP IV The Job 
A. Review the list of Alumni—note the amount sub­scribed by each. 
B. Review the list of former students. IMPORTANT NOTE: The cooperation of Local Clubs should be enlisted by City Committees—the club may consider making contacts with all graduates and non-graduates. 
C. Review the list of "Friends of The University." The Notre Dame magazine for friends of the University has just been mailed to all alumni, parents and other friends. 
D. A list of "friends" should be submitted as prospects by the local committees, so that they, too, may re­ceive the Notre Dame magazine. 
STEPV Keporting All remittances should be sent by the City Committee or donor directly to "The University of Notre Dame Foundation, Main Building, Notre Dame, Indiana." A receipt will be sent to each donor by the President of The University. 
STEP VI Summary In Review 
1.
 Make your Committee representative. 

2.
 Choose and enlist your personnel. 

3.
 Brief your personneL 

4.
 Make full use of all educational materiaL 

5.
 See individually each person to be called upon. (There is no substitute for personal contacts.) 

6.
 Set a high standard. 

7.
 Report progress. 


8.
 Follow through until the job is done.

Note that the 6% decrease in the number of alumni gifts imder $10.00 resulted in a 5% increase in gifts in the next higher bracket. Also, alumni gifts of $100.00 and over represent 5% of this year's gifts in comparison with 3% last year.

Same as Increase Decrease New Before Total At end of first quarter, 1948 6% 4% 78% 12% 100% Through July 5, 1948 5% 5% 80% 10% 100% 
Particularly noteworthy in the alumni table is the fact that 52% of the Alumni either have increased their gift this year over last year or are alumni who did not contribute at all last year. 
Of particular note in the Friends' table is that 80% of the Friends' Gifts in 1948 are gifts from friends of The University who have never before contributed to The University of Notre Dame. 
"Higher education is becoming mass education. No longer are only a few institutions looking to a few men of wealth for phil­anthropic support There are many institutions. Their needs are 
large, and incomes have been leveled so that the flow of phil­anthropy in the future must come in large measure from the lower income groups if philanthropy is to play as significant a part in finance of higher education as it has in the past. 
"The problem of acquainting philanthropists with institu­tional needs was met in the past by energetic and persuasive presidents, trustees, and deans. But, because of the changes in the sources of philanthropy, Davis expresses the opinion that institutions must henceforth present their needs and opportuni­ties with the aid of highly organized public relations depart­ments, and address their pleas to the public by radio, pageants, pamphlets, newspaper articles and all other ways resorted to by advertisers. These are the methods that reach the average citizen." From "Financing the Future of Higher Education," 
by THAD LEWIS HUNGATE.

NO GIFT TOO SMALL NO GIFT TOO LARGE 
40 

The Beqoest and Liviig Trist Cmiittee 
The importance of the will-and-bequest program to the Foundation can best be stated by quoting a few paragraphs from the July, 1948, issue of the American Alumni Council News: 
"Publicly announced gifts in eight large cities showed that $232,191,062 was given or left by will by private philanthropists last year, according to a report issued in January by John Price Jones Corjporation of New York. This represents a decline of 15.8 percent from the $275,853,339 in 1946. 
"Gifts by bequests in the cities studied amounted to $37,157,798 in 1947 as compared to $26,408,049 in the previous year. This represents an increase of 40.7 percent. 
"Although a one-year comparison does not establish a trend, neverthe­less, the above figures indicate the importance of bequ^ts to a well organ­ized fund program." 
The brochure on wills, trusts, etc.—entitled The Substance of Things Hoped For, a Guide for Benefactors of The University of Notre Dame—is in the printers' hands at the present time and circulation will begin in the near future. 
The following is a list of the Bequest and Living Trust Committees that have already been formed and are fimctioning:

To the "1948 MANUAL AND GUIDE FOR CITY COMMIT­TEES AND ALUMNI" (Foundation supplement to the March issue of The Notre Dame Alumnus) the above award was presented at the Ann Arbor Convention of the American Alumni Council on July 15. Judges selected the Notre Dame Manual from among the magazines of 182 other colleges and universities entered in the competitions. 
This September, 1948, Manual is a Supplement to the September, 1948, Issue of The Notre Dome Alumnus, Vol. 26, No. 5.
